- 5. Let  $|\phi\rangle$  be the inner state of  $C_{\lambda}^*$  at this point, compute  $\operatorname{ct}_{|\phi\rangle} \leftarrow \operatorname{Spooky.QEnc}(\operatorname{pk}_1, |\phi\rangle)$ .
- 6. For each  $i \in I$ , compute  $\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{td})} \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{Enc}(k_i, \cdot), \mathsf{ct}_i)$  and  $\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})} \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})})$  where  $\mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}$  is computed as specified in the protocol.
- 7. Let  $C_{\mathsf{Final}}^*$  be the quantum circuit (derived from the adversary) that, on input the ciphertexts  $\{\mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{td})}, \mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}\}_{i \in I}$  and the quantum state of the adversary, computes the messages of the corrupted parties (corresponding to Step 7 of the protocol) and the updated quantum state of the adversary. Compute the following spooky evaluation procedure:

$$(\bar{\mathsf{ct}}_1, \dots, \bar{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{|\phi\rangle}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}((\mathsf{pk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{pk}_\ell), \mathsf{C}^*_{\mathsf{Final}}, (\mathsf{ct}_{|\phi\rangle}, \{\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{td})}, \tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}\}_{i \in I}))$$

where each  $\bar{\mathsf{ct}}_i = (\bar{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(1)}, \dots, \bar{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell)})$  are classical ciphertexts encrypted under  $\mathsf{pk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{pk}_\ell$ .

8. For all  $i \in I$  compute

$$(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(1)}, \dots, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell)}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}\left((\mathsf{pk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{pk}_\ell), \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}\left(\mathsf{dk}_i, \bigoplus_{j=1}^\ell \cdot \right), \bar{\mathsf{ct}}_i\right).$$

9. For all  $i \in [\ell]$  define  $C_{\mathsf{Nest},i}$  to be the classical circuit that, on input a set of strings  $\{y_j\}_{j \in [i-1]}$ , a public key pk, and a ciphertext ct, computes

$$\mathsf{ct}' \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk}, y_1 \oplus \cdots \oplus y_j \oplus \cdot, \mathsf{ct}).$$

The circuit returns  $\widetilde{\mathbf{CC}}_i(\mathsf{ct}')$ .

10. For all  $i \in [\ell, ..., 2]$  compute iteratively

$$(\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk},i},\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{m,i}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}((\mathsf{pk}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{pk}_{i-1}),\mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{Nest},i}(\cdot,\mathsf{pk}_i,\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(i)}),(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(1)},\ldots,\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(i-1)}))$$

and for all  $j \in [i-1]$  update the variables

$$(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_j^{(1)}, \dots, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_j^{(i-1)}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}((\mathsf{pk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{pk}_{i-1}), \mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{Rec}, i}[\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_j^{(i)}], (\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_j^{(1)}, \dots, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_j^{(i-1)}, \widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk}, i}))$$

where  $C_{\text{Rec},i}[\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_j^{(i)}]$  is the circuit that takes as input 2i-2 strings  $(z_1,\ldots,z_{i-1})$  and  $(s_1,\ldots,s_{i-1})$  and computes

$$\bigoplus_{k=1}^{i-1} z_k \oplus \mathsf{Spooky.Dec}\left(\bigoplus_{k=1}^{i-1} s_k, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_j^{(i)}\right).$$

11. At the end of the iteration compute  $(\mathsf{sk}_1, m_1) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathbf{CC}}_1(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_1^{(1)})$ , then for all  $i \in [2, \dots, \ell]$  compute

$$\mathsf{sk}_i \leftarrow \bigoplus_{k=1}^{i-1} \mathsf{Spooky.Dec}(\mathsf{sk}_k, \tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk},i}^{(k)})$$

and

$$m_i \leftarrow \bigoplus_{k=1}^{i-1} \mathsf{Spooky.Dec}(\mathsf{sk}_k, \tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{m,i}^{(k)}).$$

{38}------------------------------------------------

12. Use the extracted keys  $(\mathsf{sk}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{sk}_\ell)$  to decrypt the state of the adversary from  $\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{|\phi\rangle}$  and the ciphertexts  $(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_1^{(\mathsf{SFE})},\ldots,\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell^{(\mathsf{SFE})})$  from the ciphertexts  $(\bar{\mathsf{ct}}_1,\ldots,\bar{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell)$  as defined in Step 7 of the extractor. Return the transcript together with the state and the extracted messages  $(m_1,\ldots,m_\ell)$ .

We are now going to show that the transcript output by the extractor is computationally indistinguishable (with respect to compliant distinguishers) from that resulting from the real execution of the protocol. We do this by defining a sequence of hybrid distributions (for all  $i \in I$ ) where we modify the interaction with the i-th corrupted party. In some of the following hybrids, the simulator inefficiently extracts the messages  $m_i$  and the trapdoors  $td_i$  from the messages  $(c_i^{(msg)}, c_i^{(td)})$  of the corrupted parties. Note that this implies that each distribution is not necessarily computable in polynomial time. However, these hybrids should be thought of as mental experiments, which are going to be helpful in arguing about the indistinguishability of the simulator (which instead runs in strict quantum polynomial time).

- $\mathcal{H}_0$ :  $\{VIEW^{\mathsf{msg}}_{\mathsf{C}^*_{\lambda}}(\langle\mathsf{R},\mathsf{C}^*_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}),\{\mathsf{C}_i(m_{i,\lambda})\}_{i\notin I}\rangle(1^{\lambda},1^n)\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$ . Recall that this distribution includes the messages  $\{m_i\}_{i\in I}$  committed by the transcript (if they exist).
- $\mathcal{H}_{i,1}$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_0$  except that  $\operatorname{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}$  is computed as SFE.Enc( $\operatorname{dk}_i, (0^\lambda, 0^\lambda)$ ).
- $\mathcal{H}_{i,2}$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_{i,1}$  except that  $c_i^{(key)}$  is computed as a commiment to  $0^{\lambda}$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_{i,3}$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_{i,2}$  except that  $\operatorname{ct}_i^{(\operatorname{td})}$  is computed as  $\operatorname{Enc}(k_i,\operatorname{td}_i)$ , where  $\operatorname{td}_i$  is extracted (inefficiently) from  $\operatorname{c}_i^{(\operatorname{td})}$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_{i,4}$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_{i,3}$  except that  $c_i^{(key)}$  is computed as a commitment to  $k_i$ , as specified in the protocol.
- $\mathcal{H}_{i,5}$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_{i,4}$  except that  $\operatorname{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}$  is computed as SFE.Enc( $\operatorname{dk}_i,(k_i,r_i)$ ), as specified in the protocol.

We then define the last hybrid below. Note that the distribution induced by this hybrid is computable in (quantum) polynomial time.

•  $\mathcal{H}_6$ : This is the output of the extractor as described above.

Now we argue indistinguishability between each hybrid. All reductions for distinguishers between hybrids below may receive the state of C\* after Step 1 along with the corresponding committed values  $\{m_i, \mathsf{td}_i\}_{i\in I}$  (if they exist) as non-uniform advice. Also, we only consider distinguishers that succeed with non-negligible probability, even conditioned on the event that the transcript received is *explainable*, i.e., each message lies in the support of the corresponding algorithm. This is because we consider only distinguishers that are *compliant*, i.e., they output 0 with overwhelming probability if the transcript is not explainable.

- $\mathcal{H}_0 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_{i,1}$ : This follows from the quantum input privacy of the SFE protocol. The reduction takes the transcript of the protocol after executing Step 1 (including the state of the adversary and the messages  $\{m_i\}_{i\in I}$ ) as non-uniform advice and continues to run the protocol honestly through Step 5. It then sets (0,0) and  $(k_i,r_i)$  as the challenge messages for SFE. In Step 6, the reduction sets  $\mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})} = \mathsf{ct}^*$ , where  $\mathsf{ct}^*$  is the challenge ciphertext. The rest of the protocol proceeds without changes. The reduction returns whatever the distinguisher returns.
  - Clearly if  $\mathsf{ct}^* = \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{dk}_i, (0, 0))$ , then the distribution is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_{i,2}$ . On the other hand if  $\mathsf{ct}^* = \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{dk}_i, (k_i, r_i))$ , then the distribution induced by the reduction is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_{i,1}$ . This implies that the two hybrids are computationally indistinguishable.
- $\mathcal{H}_{i,1} \approx_c \mathcal{H}_{i,2}$ : This follows from an invocation of the (non-uniform) quantum computational hiding of the commitment scheme.
- $\mathcal{H}_{i,2} \approx_c \mathcal{H}_{i,3}$ : This follows from a (non-uniform) reduction to the quantum semantic security of the secret-key encryption scheme, where  $\mathsf{td}_i$  (together with the transcript so far and the messages  $\{m_i\}_{i\in I}$ ) is given as non-uniform advice to the reduction.

{39}------------------------------------------------

- Hi,<sup>3</sup> ≈<sup>c</sup> Hi,4: Same argument as Hi,<sup>1</sup> ≈<sup>c</sup> Hi,2.
- Hi,<sup>4</sup> ≈<sup>c</sup> Hi,5: Same argument as H<sup>0</sup> ≈<sup>c</sup> Hi,1.
- Hi,<sup>5</sup> ≈<sup>s</sup> H6: We are going to argue that, conditioned on the event that the messages of the corrupted parties are explainable, the two hybrids are identical, except if an error in the evaluation (and consequently in the decryption) of the AFS-spooky encryption scheme occurs. Once that is estabilshed, statistical indistinguishability follows from the correctness of the AFS-spooky encryption scheme (which holds for *all choices* of the random coins used in the setup, key generation, and encryption algorithms). To substantiate this claim, recall that

$$(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{|\phi\rangle}, \bar{\mathsf{ct}}_1, \dots, \bar{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell) \leftarrow \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}((\mathsf{pk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{pk}_\ell), \mathsf{C}^*_{\mathsf{Final}}, (\mathsf{ct}_{|\phi\rangle}, \{\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{td})}, \tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}\}_{i \in I}))$$

where

$$\begin{split} \tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})} &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}) \\ &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{dk}_i, (k_i, r_i))) \end{split}$$

and

$$\begin{split} \tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{td})} &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{Enc}(k_i, \cdot), \mathsf{ct}_i) \\ &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{Enc}(k_i, \cdot), \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{td}_i)) \\ &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{Enc}(k_i, \mathsf{td}_i)). \end{split}$$

Therefore, by definition of C ∗ Final we have that for all i ∈ [`] and j ∈ [`]

$$\bar{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(j)} = \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_j, x_i^{(j)})$$

such that

$$\bigoplus_{j=1}^{\ell} x_i^{(j)} = \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})} = \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{C}[\mathsf{c}_i^{(\mathsf{key})}, \mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{td})}, \mathsf{td}_i, \mathsf{lk}_i], \mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})}).$$

Recall that

$$(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(1)}, \dots, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell)}) = \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}\left((\mathsf{pk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{pk}_\ell), \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}\left(\mathsf{dk}_i, \bigoplus_{j=1}^\ell \cdot\right), \bar{\mathsf{ct}}_i\right)$$

and therefore for all <sup>i</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [`] and <sup>j</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [`] we have that ct<sup>b</sup> (j) <sup>i</sup> = Spooky.Enc(pk<sup>j</sup> , y (j) i ) such that

$$\begin{split} \bigoplus_{j=1}^{\ell} y_i^{(j)} &= \mathsf{SFE.Dec}\left(\mathsf{dk}_i, \bigoplus_{j=1}^{\ell} x_i^{(j)}\right) \\ &= \mathsf{SFE.Dec}\left(\mathsf{dk}_i, \mathsf{SFE.Eval}(\mathsf{C}[\mathsf{c}_i^{(\mathsf{key})}, \mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{td})}, \mathsf{td}_i, \mathsf{lk}_i], \mathsf{ct}_i^{(\mathsf{SFE})})\right) \\ &= \mathsf{lk}_i \end{split}$$

by the perfect correctness of the SFE protocol. Now recall that

$$(\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk},\ell},\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{m,\ell}) = \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}((\mathsf{pk}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{pk}_{\ell-1}),\mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{Nest},\ell}(\cdot,\mathsf{pk}_\ell,\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell^{(\ell)}),(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell^{(1)},\ldots,\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell^{(\ell-1)}))$$

{40}------------------------------------------------

which implies that the two ciphertexts encode the output of the obfuscated program  $\widetilde{\mathbf{CC}}_{\ell}(\mathsf{ct}'_{\ell})$  where

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{ct}'_\ell &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk}_\ell, y_\ell^{(1)} \oplus \cdots \oplus y_\ell^{(\ell-1)} \oplus \cdot, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_\ell^{(\ell)}) \\ &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_\ell, y_\ell^{(1)} \oplus \cdots \oplus y_\ell^{(\ell)}) \\ &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_\ell, \mathsf{lk}_\ell). \end{split}$$

By the perfect correctness of the compute-and-compare obfuscation, the two variables  $(\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk},\ell},\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{m,\ell})$  are AFS-spooky encryptions of  $(\mathsf{sk}_\ell,m_\ell)$ , under  $(\mathsf{pk}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{pk}_\ell)$ . This implies that the variables  $(\hat{\mathsf{ct}}_1,\ldots,\hat{\mathsf{ct}}_{\ell-1})$  are correctly updated to

$$\begin{split} \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i &= (\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(1)}, \dots, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell-1)}) \\ &= \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Eval}((\mathsf{pk}_1, \dots, \mathsf{pk}_{\ell-1}), \mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{Rec},\ell}[\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell)}], (\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(1)}, \dots, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell-1)}, \widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk},\ell})) \end{split}$$

where  $\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(j)} = \mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_j, \widetilde{y}_i^{(j)})$  such that

$$\begin{split} \bigoplus_{j=1}^{\ell-1} \tilde{y}_i^{(j)} &= \bigoplus_{k=1}^{\ell-1} z_k \oplus \mathsf{Spooky.Dec} \left( \bigoplus_{k=1}^{\ell-1} s_k, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell)} \right) \\ &= \bigoplus_{k=1}^{\ell-1} y_i^{(k)} \oplus \mathsf{Spooky.Dec} \left( \mathsf{sk}_\ell, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_i^{(\ell)} \right) \\ &= y_i^{(1)} \oplus \cdots \oplus y_i^{(\ell)} \\ &= \mathsf{lk}_i. \end{split}$$

by the definition of  $C_{Rec,\ell}[\widehat{ct}_i^{(\ell)}]$ . Recursively applying the above procedure, we obtain that

$$\widetilde{\mathbf{CC}}_1(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_1) = \widetilde{\mathbf{CC}}_1(\mathsf{Spooky}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_1,\mathsf{lk}_1)) = (\mathsf{sk}_1,m_1)$$

by the perfect correctness of the compute-and-compare obfuscation. It follows that the extractor successfully recomputes  $\mathsf{sk}_1$ , which allows it to iteratively recover  $(\mathsf{sk}_2,\ldots,\mathsf{sk}_\ell)$  from  $(\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk},2},\ldots,\tilde{\mathsf{ct}}_{\mathsf{sk},\ell})$ . Consequently, the decrypted transcript, the (possibly quantum) state of the adversary, and the messages  $(m_1,\ldots,m_\ell)$  are distributed identically as in the previous hybrid, conditioned on the fact that no error occurs during the evaluation algorithm.

### <span id="page-40-0"></span>6 Quantum-Secure Multi-Verifier Zero-Knowledge

In this section, we use standard techniques to derive a multi-verifier zero-knowledge protocol from our multi-committer extractable commitment. We follow the approach given in [GK96] to upgrade a commitchallenge-response  $\Sigma$  protocol to a full-fledged zero-knowledge protocol in constant rounds. In particular, the (multiple) verifiers will each commit to their challenge before the  $\Sigma$  protocol is executed, using our multi-committer extractable commitment scheme. A simulator will then be able to extract the challenge from all verifiers *simultaneously* and proceed to simulate each  $\Sigma$  protocol.

As in [BS20], a couple of subtleties arise in the proof. First, the extractable commitment guarantee does not hold against arbitrary malicious verifiers, as captured by our notion of simulation indistinguishability against *compliant* distinguishers. Thus, we have the verifier attach a witness indistinguishable proof (WI) that it acted explainably during the commitment phase, and indeed committed to the challenge that is

{41}------------------------------------------------

sent during the  $\Sigma$  protocol. However, in the proof of soundness, the verifier's initial commitment must be switched to a commitment to 0, since the reduction will receive the  $\Sigma$  protocol challenge from its challenger. This requires the verifier to prove a different statement under the WI, which must only be possible when interacting with a cheating prover. Details can be found in the description of Protocol 3.

**Simulation Strategy.** Following [BS20], we construct a zero-knowledge simulator that makes use of two non-rewinding sub-routines. Given an arbitrary malicious (multi-)verifier  $V^*$ , we consider the following two distributions. First, consider the real distribution over the final state of  $V^*$  on interaction with the honest prover, except that any time  $V^*$  aborts, the distribution outputs only a  $\bot$  symbol. We refer to this as RealNoAbort $_\bot(V^*)$ . Next, consider the real distribution except that any time  $V^*$  *does not* abort, the distribution outputs only a  $\bot$  symbol. We refer to this as RealAbort $_\bot(V^*)$ .

As a stepping stone towards proving zero-knowledge, we construct an entirely straight-line simulator  $SimNoAbort_{\perp}$  such that  $SimNoAbort_{\perp}(V^*)$  is indistiguishable from  $RealNoAbort_{\perp}(V^*)$ . By entirely straight-line, we mean that not only does  $SimNoAbort_{\perp}$  not rewind  $V^*$ , it never even re-starts  $V^*$  from the beginning. Analogously, we also construct a simulator  $SimAbort_{\perp}$  such that  $SimAbort_{\perp}(V^*)$  is indistiguishable from  $RealAbort_{\perp}(V^*)$ , and  $SimAbort_{\perp}$  is entirely straight-line.

Now, we combine the above simulators into a straight-line simulator  $\mathsf{SimComb}_\perp$  that succeeds with probability negligibly close to 1/2.  $\mathsf{SimComb}_\perp$  simply chooses uniformly at random whether to run  $\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_\perp$  or  $\mathsf{SimAbort}_\perp$  and outputs the resulting view if the sub-routine is successful and  $\perp$  otherwise. Finally, we invoke the Watrous rewinding lemma to amplify the success probability of  $\mathsf{SimComb}_\perp$ , resulting in the final simulator  $\mathsf{Sim}$ .

We will actually make use of the sub-routines  $SimNoAbort_{\perp}$  and  $SimAbort_{\perp}$  explicitly in later sections, where the entirely straight-line nature of these procedures will be useful. In particular, we use both simulators in constructing non-malleable commitments (Section 7) and just  $SimNoAbort_{\perp}$  in the coin-flipping protocol in Section 8 (since we define an alternate/simpler abort generation procedure in that protocol).

#### <span id="page-41-0"></span>6.1 Definition

**Definition 6.1** (Quantum-Secure Multi-Verifier Zero-Knowledge Argument for NP). A quantum-secure multiverifier zero-knowledge argument for a language  $\mathcal{L} \in \text{NP}$  is a pair (P, V) of classical PPT interactive Turing machines. P interacts with n copies  $\{V_i\}_{i\in[n]}$  of V (who do not interact with each other) on common input  $1^{\lambda}$  and  $1^n$ , with each  $V_i$  additionally taking an input  $x_i \in \mathcal{L}$ , and P additionally taking inputs  $\{x_i, w_i \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}(x_i)\}_{i\in[n]}$ . At the end of the interaction, each  $V_i$  outputs a bit, indicating whether it accepts or rejects.

1. Perfect Completeness: For any  $\lambda, n \in \mathbb{N}, i \in [n], x \in \mathcal{L} \cap \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , and  $w \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}(x)$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{OUT}_{\mathsf{V}_i}\langle\mathsf{P}(x,w),\mathsf{V}_i(x)\rangle(1^\lambda,1^n)=1]=1.$$

2. **Quantum Computational Soundness:** For any non-uniform quantum polynomial-size prover  $P^* = \{P_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mu(\cdot)$  such that for all  $\lambda, n \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $i \in [n]$ , and any  $x \in \{0, 1\}^{\lambda} \setminus \mathcal{L}$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{OUT}_{\mathsf{V}_i} \langle \mathsf{P}_{\lambda}^*(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathsf{V}_i(x) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, 1^n) = 1] \le \mu(\lambda).$$

3. **Quantum Computational Zero-Knowledge:** There exists a quantum expected polynomial-time simulator Sim such that for any non-uniform quantum polynomial-size adversary  $V^* = \{V_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  representing a subset of n verifiers, namely,  $\{V_i\}_{i \in I}$  for some set  $I \subseteq [n]$ ,

$$\begin{split} & \left\{ \mathsf{VIEW}_{\mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}} \left\langle \begin{array}{l} \mathsf{P}(\{x_{i}, w_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}), \\ \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}(\{x_{i}\}_{i \in I}, \rho_{\lambda}), \\ \{\mathsf{V}_{i}(x_{i})\}_{i \notin I} \end{array} \right\rangle (1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}) \right\}_{\lambda, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}, \{w_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}} \\ \approx_{c} \{ \mathsf{Sim}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in n}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}, \rho_{\lambda}) \}_{\lambda, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}, \{w_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}}, \end{split}$$

where  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}, x_i \in \mathcal{L} \cap \{0,1\}^{\lambda}, w_i \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}(x_i)$ .

{42}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-42-0"></span>**6.2 Construction**

**Ingredients:** All of the following are assumed to be quantum-secure.

- A non-interactive perfectly-binding commitment Com.
- A multi-committer extractable commitment eCom = (eCom.C, eCom.R).
- A WI proof system WI = (WI.P, WI.V).
- A sigma protocol for NP Σ = (Σ.P, Σ.V).

*Remark* 6.1*.* Observe that since the sigma protocol Σ is public-coin, Protocol [3](#page-42-1) is publicly-verifiable. That is, any third party, upon observing the transcript of interaction between P and V, can deduce whether V accepted or not. This fact will be used in Section [8.](#page-59-0)

### **Protocol [3](#page-42-1)**

<span id="page-42-1"></span>**Common input:** 1 <sup>λ</sup> and 1 n.

Vi **'s additional input:** x<sup>i</sup> ∈ L.

P**'s additional input:** {x<sup>i</sup> , w<sup>i</sup> ∈ RL(xi)}i∈[n] .

- 1. For each i ∈ [n], P computes and sends c<sup>i</sup> ← Com(1<sup>λ</sup> , wi) to V<sup>i</sup>
- 2. Each V<sup>i</sup> computes a challenge β<sup>i</sup> ← Σ.V1(1<sup>|</sup>xi<sup>|</sup> ). Then, P and {Vi}i∈[n] interact, with P taking the role of eCom.R and V<sup>i</sup> taking the role of eCom.Ci(βi), to produce {τi}i∈[n] ← heCom.R, {eCom.Ci(βi)}i∈[n]i(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 n).

.

- 3. For each i ∈ [n], P computes (α<sup>i</sup> ,sti) ← Σ.P1(x<sup>i</sup> , wi) and sends α<sup>i</sup> to V<sup>i</sup> .
- 4. Each V<sup>i</sup> sends β<sup>i</sup> .
- 5. For each i ∈ [n], P and V<sup>i</sup> interact (in parallel) with P taking the role of WI.V and V<sup>i</sup> taking the role of WI.P to give P a WI proof that
  - τ<sup>i</sup> is explainable, and opens to β<sup>i</sup> ,
  - **OR,** c<sup>i</sup> opens to a non-witness z<sup>i</sup> ∈ R/ <sup>L</sup>(xi).
- 6. For each i ∈ [n], P and V<sup>i</sup> interact (in parallel) with P taking the role of WI.P and V<sup>i</sup> taking the role of WI.V to give V<sup>i</sup> a WI proof that
  - c<sup>i</sup> opens to some string z<sup>i</sup> ,
  - **OR,** x<sup>i</sup> ∈ L.
- 7. For each i ∈ [n], P computes and sends γ<sup>i</sup> = Σ.P2(x<sup>i</sup> , w<sup>i</sup> ,st<sup>i</sup> , α<sup>i</sup> , βi) to V<sup>i</sup> .
- 8. Each V<sup>i</sup> accepts if Σ.V2(x<sup>i</sup> , α<sup>i</sup> , β<sup>i</sup> , γi) = 1.

Figure 3: A constant-round quantum-secure multi-verifier zero-knowledge argument for L ∈ NP.

{43}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-43-0"></span>6.3 Soundness

**Lemma 6.1.** Protocol 3 has quantum computational soundness.

*Proof.* Assume towards contradication that there exists a non-uniform quantum polynomial-size prover  $\mathsf{P}^* = \{\mathsf{P}_\lambda^*, \rho_\lambda^*\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  that with noticeable probability, convinces  $\mathsf{V}_i$  to accept on input instances  $\{x_\lambda\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  where  $|x_\lambda| = \lambda$  and  $x_\lambda \notin \mathcal{L}$ . Let  $\mathsf{V} = \mathsf{V}_i$ , and by averaging, we can assume that  $\mathsf{P}_\lambda^*$  sends a fixed first message  $\mathsf{c}_\lambda$  to  $\mathsf{V}$ . Furthermore, since  $\mathsf{P}_\lambda^*$  succeeds in convincing  $\mathsf{V}$  to accept instances  $x_\lambda \notin \mathcal{L}$  with noticeable probability, the statistical soundness of the WI in Step 6 of the protocol implies that  $\mathsf{c}_\lambda$  must be a well-formed commitment, that is,  $\mathsf{c}_\lambda = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda, z_\lambda; s_\lambda)$  for some  $(z_\lambda, s_\lambda)$ . Now, consider the following sequence of computationally indistinguishable hybrid distributions.

- $\mathcal{H}_0$ : {VIEW<sub>P\*</sub>  $\langle P^*(\rho_\lambda), V \rangle (1^\lambda, x_\lambda) \}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_1$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_0$  except that V uses  $(z_{\lambda}, s_{\lambda})$  as the witness for the second part of the WI statement given in Step 5 of the protocol.  $\mathcal{H}_0 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_1$  follows from the witness indistinguishability of WI, where the reduction is given  $(z_{\lambda}, s_{\lambda})$  as non-uniform advice.
- $\mathcal{H}_2$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except that in Step 2, V takes the role of eCom.C( $0^{\lambda}$ ) rather than eCom.C( $\beta$ ).  $\mathcal{H}_1 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_2$  follows from the computational hiding of eCom.

Using P\*, we construct a cheating prover  $\Sigma.P^* = \{\Sigma.P_{\lambda}^*, \Sigma.\rho_{\lambda}^*\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  for the sigma protocol. The non-uniform advice  $\Sigma.\rho_{\lambda}^*$  is generated as follows. Run P\*( $\rho_{\lambda}^*$ ) until it outputs its first message  $c_{\lambda}$ . Extract from  $c_{\lambda}$  the message  $z_{\lambda}$  committed and the corresponding opening  $s_{\lambda}$  and define the resulting advice to consist of the state of P\* at this point, along with  $(z_{\lambda}, s_{\lambda})$ .

### $\Sigma.\mathsf{P}^*_{\lambda}(\Sigma.\rho^*_{\lambda})$ :

- 1. Interact with  $P_{\lambda}^*$ , taking the role of eCom. $C(0^{\lambda})$ .
- 2. Continue running  $P_{\lambda}^*$ , obtaining the message  $\alpha$ .
- 3. Send  $\alpha$  to  $\Sigma$ .V, receive  $\beta$ , and send  $\beta$  to  $\mathsf{P}_{\lambda}^*$ .
- 4. Interact with  $P_{\lambda}^*$  to give  $P_{\lambda}^*$  a WI proof as in Step 5 of the protocol, using witness  $(z_{\lambda}, s_{\lambda})$ .
- 5. Interact with  $P_{\lambda}^*$  to receive a WI proof from  $P_{\lambda}^*$  as in Step 6 of the protocol.
- 6. Continue running  $P_{\lambda}^*$ , obtaining the message  $\gamma$ , and send  $\gamma$  to  $\Sigma$ .V.

Now note that  $P_{\lambda}^{*}$ 's view in this interaction is exactly  $\mathcal{H}_{2}$ . Thus, since  $P^{*}$  succeeds in convincing V to accept with noticeable probability, and  $\mathcal{H}_{0} \approx_{c} \mathcal{H}_{2}$ , it must be the case that  $\Sigma$ .V accepts with noticeable probability, a contradiction.

### <span id="page-43-1"></span>6.4 Zero-Knowledge

<span id="page-43-2"></span>**Theorem 6.1.** Protocol 3 is quantum computational zero-knowledge.

*Proof.* We begin by describing the two sub-routines  $SimNoAbort_{\perp}$  and  $SimAbort_{\perp}$  mentioned above. Then we combine them into  $SimComb_{\lambda}$ , which we use to derive the final simulator Sim.

$$\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \{x_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda})$$
:

1. Set  $\rho_{\lambda}$  to be the inner state of  $V_{\lambda}^*$ . For each  $i \in I$ , compute and send  $c_i \leftarrow \text{Com}(1^{\lambda}, 0)$  to  $V_{\lambda}^*$ .

{44}------------------------------------------------

2. Let V.eCom $_{\lambda}^{*}$  be the portion of V $_{\lambda}^{*}$  that interacts with P in Step 2 above. Note that its state at the beginning of this interaction is  $\rho_{\lambda}$ ,  $\{c_{i}\}_{i\in I}$ . Compute

$$(\{\tau_i\}_{i\in I},\mathsf{st},\{\beta_i'\}_{i\in I})\leftarrow\mathsf{eCom}.\mathsf{Ext}(1^\lambda,1^n,I,\mathsf{V}.\mathsf{eCom}_\lambda^*,(\rho_\lambda,\{\mathsf{c}_i\}_{i\in I})).$$

If eCom.Ext produced an abort transcript, then halt and return  $\bot$ . Otherwise continue, setting st to be the inner state of  $V_{\lambda}^*$ .

- 3. For all  $i \in I$ , compute  $(\alpha_i, \gamma_i) \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Sim}(x_i, \beta_i')$  and send  $\alpha_i$  to  $\mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^*$ .
- 4.  $V_{\lambda}^*$  returns  $\{\beta_i\}_{i\in I}$ .
- 5. Take the role of the honest prover WI.P in the |I| WI proofs that  $V_{\lambda}^*$  gives. If  $V_{\lambda}^*$  fails to prove any of the statements, then halt and output  $\bot$ .
- 6. Give  $V_{\lambda}^*$  a total of |I| WI proofs using the |I| witnesses that show  $\{c_i\}_{i\in I}$  are valid commitments. Then, send  $\{\gamma_i\}_{i\in I}$  to  $V_{\lambda}^*$ .
- 7. Output the inner state of  $V_{\lambda}^*$ .

 $\mathsf{SimAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \{x_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}):$ 

- 1. Set  $\rho_{\lambda}$  to be the inner state of  $V_{\lambda}^*$ .
- 2. Interact with  $V_{\lambda}^*$  as the honest prover until the end of Step 6 of the protocol, with exactly 3 differences:
  - The commitments  $c_i$  in Step 1 are to 0 rather than  $w_i$ .
  - The messages  $\alpha_i$  sent in Step 3 are generated by the simulator of the sigma protocol,  $(\alpha_i, \gamma_i) \leftarrow \Sigma.\text{Sim}(x_i, 0^{\lambda})$ .
  - In Step 6, the witnesses used for the WI proofs are for the first statement (that c<sub>i</sub> is a valid commitment).
- 3. If at some point during the interaction  $V_{\lambda}^*$  either aborts or fails in one of its WI proofs, halt and output  $V_{\lambda}^*$ 's inner state. Otherwise, output  $\bot$ .

 $\mathsf{SimComb}_{\bot}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}, \rho_{\lambda}) \text{: With probability } 1/2 \text{, execute } \mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\bot}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}, \rho_{\lambda}) \text{ and otherwise execute } \mathsf{SimAbort}_{\bot}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}, \rho_{\lambda}).$ 

 $\mathsf{Sim}(1^\lambda, 1^n, I, \{x_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}^*_\lambda, \rho_\lambda)$ : Let  $\overline{\mathsf{SimComb}}_\bot(\cdot) \coloneqq \mathsf{SimComb}_\bot(1^\lambda, 1^n, I, \{x_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}^*_\lambda, \cdot)$  be the circuit  $\mathsf{SimComb}_\bot$  with all inputs hard-coded except for  $\rho_\lambda$ , and output  $\mathsf{R}(\overline{\mathsf{SimComb}}_\bot, \rho_\lambda, \lambda)$ , where  $\mathsf{R}$  is the algorithm from Lemma 3.1.

Next, we introduce some notation. For  $V^* = \{V_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ , let  $\mathsf{Real}(V^*)$  denote

$$\left\{ \mathsf{VIEW}_{\mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}} \left\langle \begin{array}{l} \mathsf{P}(\{x_{i}, w_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}), \\ \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}(\{x_{i}\}_{i \in I}, \rho_{\lambda}), \\ \{\mathsf{V}_{i}(x_{i})\}_{i \notin I} \end{array} \right\rangle (1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}) \right\}_{\lambda, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}, \{w_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}}$$

for  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}, x_i \in \mathcal{L} \cap \{0,1\}^{\lambda}, w_i \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}(x_i)$ , and let

- RealNoAbort $_{\perp}(V^*)$  be the distribution Real $(V^*)$ , except that whenever an abort occurs, the distribution outputs  $\perp$ , and
- RealAbort $_{\perp}(V^*)$  be the distribution Real $(V^*)$ , except that if an abort *does not* occur, the distribution outputs  $\perp$ .

{45}------------------------------------------------

We continue by proving two lemmas that will be useful on their own in later sections, and will also be useful in proving the quantum computational zero-knowledge of ZK.

<span id="page-45-1"></span>**Lemma 6.2.** For any  $V^*$ , RealNoAbort $_{\perp}(V^*) \approx_c \mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(V^*)$ .

*Proof.* We prove this via a sequence of hybrids.

- $\mathcal{H}_0$ : RealNoAbort $_{\perp}(V^*)$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_1$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_0$  except that after Step 2, the values  $\{\beta'_i\}_{i\in I}$  committed by the transcripts  $\{\tau_i\}_{i\in I}$  are inefficiently extracted, and after Step 4, if any  $\beta'_i \neq \beta_i$ , the hybrid aborts (outputs  $\perp$ ).
- $\mathcal{H}_2$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except that the transcript of the extractable commitment is simulated. In particular, the hybrid computes

$$(\{\tau_i\}_{i\in I}, \mathsf{st}, \{\beta_i'\}_{i\in I}) \leftarrow \mathsf{eCom}.\mathsf{Ext}(1^\lambda, 1^n, I, \mathsf{V.eCom}_\lambda^*, (\rho_\lambda, \{\mathsf{c}_i\}_{i\in I})),$$

and proceeds to run the verifier with inner state st.

- $\mathcal{H}_3$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_2$  except that the equality checks introduced in  $\mathcal{H}_1$  are removed, and the sigma protocol is simulated. In particular, for each  $i \in I$ , the hybrid computes  $(\alpha_i, \gamma_i) \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Sim}(x_i, \beta_i')$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_4$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_3$  except that each commitment for  $i \in I$  in the first message sent by the prover is  $c_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Com}(1^{\lambda}, 0)$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_5$ : Same as  $\mathcal{H}_4$  except that the WI proofs given by the prover for  $i \in I$  are generated with witnesses showing that  $c_i$  is a valid commitment.

Observe that  $\mathcal{H}_5$  is exactly SimNoAbort $_{\perp}(V^*)$ . Now we show that each consecutive pair of hybrids is indistiguishable.

- $\mathcal{H}_0 \approx_s \mathcal{H}_1$ : This follows from the statistical soundness of the WI proved in Step 5.
- $\mathcal{H}_1 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_2$ : Assume there exists a distinguisher D for  $\mathcal{H}_1$  and  $\mathcal{H}_2$  that succeeds with non-negligible probability. We build a compliant<sup>15</sup> distinguisher D' that breaks the extractability property of eCom.

First, we fix a sequence of instance-witness pairs  $\{\{x_{i,\lambda},w_{i,\lambda}\}_{i\in I}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$ , and first messages  $\{\{c_{i,\lambda}\}_{i\in I}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$  for which D succeeds with non-negligible probability. The witnesses  $\{\{w_{i,\lambda}\}_{i\in I}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$  will be given as non-uniform advice to D'. Now, D' will take as input either the real or the simulated view with respect to committer  $\{V.eCom_{\lambda}^*,(\rho_{\lambda},\{c_{i,\lambda}\}_{i\in I})\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$ . This view includes the messages committed and the final state of the committer, which is the state of V\* after Step 2 of the protocol. D' proceeds to simulate the rest of the interaction between P and V\*, making use of the witnesses it received as non-uniform advice during Steps 3 and 7, as well as the committed messages it received from its challenger to implement the check introduced in  $\mathcal{H}_1$ . If V\* aborts or fails to prove any of the WI statements in Step 5, D' outputs 0. Otherwise, it queries D with V\*'s final view and outputs what D' outputs.

Observe that D''s advantage is equivalent to D's advantage. This follows becuase i) whenever D' queries D with a transcript, it is a faithful execution of either  $\mathcal{H}_1$  or  $\mathcal{H}_2$ , depending on whether eCom was simulated or not, and ii) whenever D' does *not* query D, it means that V\* failed to prove one of its WI statements, so D's input would have been  $\bot$ . Finally, D' is compliant by the statistical soundness of the WI.

- $\mathcal{H}_2 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_3$ : This follows from the special zero-knowledge property of  $\Sigma$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_3 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4$ : This follows from the computational hiding of Com.
- $\mathcal{H}_4 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_5$ : This follows from the witness indistinguishability of WI.

<span id="page-45-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>15</sup>Recall that such a distinguisher is guaranteed to output 0 with overwhelming probability on input any non-explainable view.

{46}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-46-0"></span>**Lemma 6.3.** *For any* V ∗ *,* RealAbort⊥(V ∗ ) ≈<sup>c</sup> SimAbort⊥(V ∗ )*.*

*Proof.* We prove this via a sequence of hybrids.

- H0: RealAbort⊥(V ∗ ). Note that if V <sup>∗</sup> has not aborted at some point during Steps 1-6 of the protocol, this distribution outputs ⊥.
- H1: Same as H<sup>0</sup> except that in Step 3, for each i ∈ I, P sends α<sup>i</sup> where (α<sup>i</sup> , γi) ← Sim(x<sup>i</sup> , 0 λ ).
- H2: Same as H<sup>1</sup> except that each commitment for i ∈ I in the first message sent by the prover is c<sup>i</sup> ← Com(1<sup>λ</sup> , 0).
- H3: Same as H<sup>2</sup> except that the WI proofs for i ∈ I given by the prover are generated with witnesses showing that c<sup>i</sup> is a valid commitment.

Observe that H<sup>3</sup> is exactly SimAbort⊥(V ∗ ). Now we show that each consecutive pair of hybrids in indistinguishable.

- H<sup>0</sup> ≈<sup>c</sup> H1: This follows from the first-message indistinguishability of Σ.
- H<sup>1</sup> ≈<sup>c</sup> H2: This follows from the computational hiding of Com.
- H<sup>2</sup> ≈<sup>c</sup> H3: This follows from the witness indistinguishability of WI.

To finish the proof of zero-knowledge, we introduce some more notation.

- Let PrAbort Real (V ∗ ) be the probability that V <sup>∗</sup> aborts in the real interaction with the honest prover.
- Let PrAbort SimNoAbort(V ∗ ) be the probability that V <sup>∗</sup> aborts in SimNoAbort<sup>⊥</sup> (i.e. the outcome is ⊥).
- Let PrAbort SimAbort(V ∗ ) be the probability that V <sup>∗</sup> aborts in SimAbort<sup>⊥</sup> (i.e. the outcome is not ⊥).
- Let SimNoAbort⊥(V ∗ ) := {SimNoAbort⊥(1<sup>λ</sup> , n, I, {xi}i∈[n] , V ∗ λ , ρλ)}λ,{xi}i∈[n] ,{wi}i∈[n] .
- Let SimAbort⊥(V ∗ ) := {SimAbort⊥(1<sup>λ</sup> , n, I, {xi}i∈[n] , V ∗ λ , ρλ)}λ,{xi}i∈[n] ,{wi}i∈[n] .
- Let SimComb⊥(V ∗ ) := {SimComb⊥(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 <sup>n</sup>, I, {xi}i∈[n] , V ∗ λ , ρλ)}λ,{xi}i∈[n] ,{wi}i∈[n] .
- Let Sim(V ∗ ) := {Sim(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 <sup>n</sup>, I, {xi}i∈[n] , V ∗ λ , ρλ)}λ,{xi}i∈[n] ,{wi}i∈[n] .
- Let RealNoAbort(V ∗ ) be the distribution Real(V ∗ ) conditioned on there not being an abort.
- Let RealAbort(V ∗ ) be the distribution Real(V ∗ ) conditioned on there being an abort.
- Let SimNoAbort(V ∗ ) be the distribution SimNoAbort<sup>⊥</sup> conditioned on there not being an abort (i.e. conditioned on the output not being ⊥).
- Let SimAbort(V ∗ ) be the distribution SimAbort<sup>⊥</sup> conditioned on there being an abort (i.e. conditioned on the output not being ⊥).
- Let SimComb(V ∗ ) be the distribution SimComb<sup>⊥</sup> conditioned on the output not being ⊥.

{47}------------------------------------------------

Following [BS20], we show that  $Real(V^*) \approx_c SimComb(V^*)$  via a sequence on hybrids. In particular, we show that

$$\begin{split} \text{Real}(\mathsf{V}^*) &\overset{(1)}{\equiv} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{Real}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{RealNoAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{Real}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{RealAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) \\ &\overset{(2)}{\approx} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{SimNoAbort}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{RealNoAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{SimAbort}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{RealAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) \\ &\overset{(3)}{\approx} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{SimNoAbort}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{SimNoAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{SimAbort}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{RealAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) \\ &\overset{(4)}{\approx} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{SimNoAbort}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{SimNoAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{\mathsf{SimAbort}}(\mathsf{V}^*)) \mathsf{SimAbort}(\mathsf{V}^*) \\ &\overset{(5)}{\approx} \mathsf{SimComb}(\mathsf{V}^*), \end{split}$$

#### where

- 1. The equality (1) follows by definition.
- 2. The indistinguishability (2) follows as a corollary of Lemma 6.2 and Lemma 6.3. Indeed, RealNoAbort $_{\perp}(V^*) \approx_c SimNoAbort_{\perp}(V^*)$  in particular implies that the difference in the probability that the verifier aborts in the real interaction versus the simulated interaction is negligible, and likewise for RealAbort $_{\perp}(V^*) \approx_c SimAbort_{\perp}(V^*)$ .
- 3. The indistinguishability (3) follows as a corollary of Lemma 6.2. This can be seen by considering two cases. First, if the probability that the verifier aborts in the real interaction is negligible, then RealNoAbort(V\*)  $\approx_c \text{SimNoAbort}(V^*)$  directly follows from Lemma 6.2, and the indistinguishability follows. Otherwise, this probability is non-negligible, meaning that RealAbort(V\*) is efficiently sampleable. Thus, a reduction to Lemma 6.2 can sample from the distribution RealAbort(V\*) whenever it receives  $\perp$  from its challenger. 16
- 4. The indistinguishability (4) follows as a corollary of Lemma 6.3 via a similar analysis as the last step.
- 5. The indistinguishability (5) follows from the definition of  $Sim(V^*)$  and the claim that the difference between  $Pr_{SimNoAbort}^{Abort}$  and  $Pr_{SimAbort}^{Abort}$  is negligible (which follows as a corollary of Lemma 6.2 and Lemma 6.3).

Finally, this implies that  $\operatorname{Real}(\mathsf{V}^*) \approx_c \operatorname{Sim}(\mathsf{V}^*)$  by applying Lemma 3.1 for each  $(\lambda, \{x_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \{w_i\}_{i \in [n]})$  with the following parameters. Set  $\mathsf{Q} \coloneqq \overline{\mathsf{SimComb}}_\perp$  as defined in the description of  $\mathsf{Sim}$ , and set  $\epsilon \coloneqq \operatorname{negl}(\lambda) + 2^{-\lambda \cdot \frac{3}{4}}, p_0 \coloneqq 1/4,$  and  $q \coloneqq 1/2$ , as described in [BS20, Proposition 3.5]. This completes the proof of quantum computational zero-knowledge.

### <span id="page-47-0"></span>7 Quantum-Secure Non-Malleable Commitments

#### <span id="page-47-1"></span>7.1 Definition

In this section, we define quantum-secure non-malleable commitments w.r.t. commitment. We consider the synchronous setting where there is a quantum man-in-the-middle adversary  $\mathsf{MIM} = \{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  interacting with a classical honest committer  $\mathcal{C}$  with tag  $\mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}$  (where  $\mathcal{C}$  commits to value v) in the left session, and interacting with classical honest receiver  $\mathcal{R}$  in the right session. The MIM uses  $\mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}$  in its interaction with  $\mathcal{R}$ . Prior to the interaction, the value v is given to  $\mathcal{C}$  as local input.

<span id="page-47-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>16</sup>A more formal analysis of this can be found in [BS20, Proposition 3.4].

{48}------------------------------------------------

Then the commit phase is executed. After obtaining an honest left message in any round, the MIM sends its own right message. And after obtaining an honest right message in any round, the MIM sends its own left message. Let View-Val<sub>MIM</sub> $_{\lambda}\langle \mathcal{C}(v), \text{MIM}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R}\rangle(1^{\lambda}, \text{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \text{tag}_{\text{MIM}})$  denote a random variable that describes the value v' committed by the MIM in the right session, jointly with the view of the MIM in the full (both left and right sessions) experiment. If the tag\_ $\mathcal{C}$  used by  $\mathcal{C}$  in the left interaction is identical to the tag<sub>MIM</sub> used by the MIM in the right interaction, then the value v' committed to in the right interaction is defined to be  $\bot$ . If the MIM sends a message that causes an honest party to abort in either the left or the right execution, then the value v' committed to in the right interaction is also defined to be  $\bot$ .

We will concern ourselves with computationally hiding and statistically binding commitments that additionally satisfy the non-malleability property defined below.

<span id="page-48-1"></span>**Definition 7.1** (Quantum-Secure Non-Malleable Commitments with respect to Commitment). For any  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$  and  $p = p(\lambda)$ , a commitment scheme  $\langle \mathcal{C}, \mathcal{R} \rangle$  is said to be quantum secure non-malleable with respect to commitment for tags in  $[\ell]$  if for every  $v_1, v_2 \in \{0, 1\}^{2p(\lambda)}$ , for every quantum polynomial-size MIM  $= \{\text{MIM}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  and every quantum polynomial-size distinguisher  $D = \{D_{\lambda}, \sigma_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\eta(\cdot)$  such that for all large enough  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , and for all  $\log_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,  $\log_{\text{MIM}} \in [\ell]$  where  $\log_{\mathcal{C}} \neq \log_{\text{MIM}}$ , the following holds:

$$\begin{split} & \left| \Pr[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda} \big( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(v_1), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \big) = 1] \\ & - \Pr[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda} \big( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(v_2), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \big) = 1] \right| = \eta(\lambda) \end{split} \tag{4}$$

We will also consider a more general setting where the MIM interacts with polynomially many committers in the left session, and a single honest receiver in the right session. For any polynomial  $n=n(\lambda)$  number of left sessions, we will let View-Val $_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}}\langle\mathcal{C}(\{v_i\}_{i\in[n]}),\mathsf{MIM}(\rho_{\lambda}),\mathcal{R}\rangle(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}},\mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}})$  denote a random variable that describes the value v' committed by the MIM in the right session, jointly with the view of the MIM in the full (both left and right sessions) experiment.

<span id="page-48-2"></span>**Definition 7.2** (Many-one Quantum-Secure Non-Malleable Commitments with respect to Commitment). For any  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ ,  $p = p(\lambda)$  and  $n = n(\lambda)$ , a commitment scheme  $\langle \mathcal{C}, \mathcal{R} \rangle$  is said to be quantum secure many-one non-malleable with respect to commitment for tags in  $[\ell]$  if for every pair of tuples  $(\{v_i^1\}_{i \in [n]}), (\{v_i^2\}_{i \in [n]}) \in \{0,1\}^{2np(\lambda)}$ , for every quantum polynomial-size MIM  $= \{\text{MIM}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  and every quantum polynomial-size distinguisher  $D = \{D_{\lambda}, \sigma_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\eta(\cdot)$  such that for all large enough  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , and for all  $(\{\text{tag}_i^{\mathcal{C}}\}_{i \in [n]})$ ,  $\text{tag}^{\text{MIM}}$  where each tag is in  $[\ell]$  such that  $\text{tag}^{\text{MIM}} \not\in \{\text{tag}_i^{\mathcal{C}}\}_{i \in [n]}$ , the following holds:

$$\left| \Pr[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda} \left( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(\{v_{i}^{1}\}_{i \in [n]}), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \right) = 1 \right] - \Pr[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda} \left( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(\{v_{i}^{2}\}_{i \in [n]}), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \right) = 1 \right] = \eta(\lambda)$$
(5)

### <span id="page-48-0"></span>7.2 Non-Malleable Commitments for Small Tags

First, we provide an overview of our scheme for tags in [N] where  $N = \lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c+1,\lambda)}$ . We will assume non-interactive perfectly binding commitments and two-message SFE which can be broken with advantage at most  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)})$  by polynomial size quantum circuits. Recall that as discussed in the technical overview, we will have the committer and receiver establish an erasure channel via a two-party input-hiding SFE. This channel will transmit the committer's value with probability  $\epsilon$ , depending on their tag. Here, we discuss our construction in more detail.

The committer on input  $m \in \{0,1\}^{p(\lambda)}$  sends a perfectly binding, computationally hiding commitment to m, denoted by  $\mathsf{Com}(m)$ . Next, the committer and receiver run an SFE execution, where the receiver input is a uniformly random  $r_1$  and committer input is m along with uniformly random  $s_1$  that is of the same length as  $r_1$ , and m is transmitted to the receiver if and only if  $s_1 = r_1$ . The length of  $r_1$  and  $s_1$  is carefully chosen so that the probability that they are equal is  $\eta^{-\mathsf{tag}}$ , where  $\eta = \lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c+1,\lambda)}$  is a small superpolynomial value. Additionally, the SFE scheme is such that evaluations of agreeing circuits are (subexponentially)

{49}------------------------------------------------

statistically close. This essentially means that no matter how a malicious committer or receiver may behave, the message m is revealed with probability close to  $\eta^{-\mathsf{tag}}$  (with an error of  $\mathsf{negl}(\eta^{\mathsf{tag}})$ ).

Now, let us consider a setting where the MIM uses  $tag_{MIM}$  and honest committer uses  $tag_{\mathcal{C}}$  such that  $tag_{MIM} < tag_{\mathcal{C}}$ . In this case, the SFE statistically hides the committed message except with probability roughly  $\eta^{-tag_{\mathcal{C}}}$ , and on the other hand, the MIM's message is revealed with probability roughly  $\eta^{-tag_{MIM}}$ , which is greater than  $\eta^{-tag_{\mathcal{C}}}$ . Intuitively, this means that any MIM that tries to maul or copy the committed message cannot succeed, at least in executions where the MIM's value was revealed but the honest committer's was not. We generalize this to all transcripts by relying on the fact that no MIM can actually tell whether the MIM's value was revealed, and therefore cannot behave any differently in transcripts where extraction occurred vs where it didn't.

Formally, we will prove that the joint distribution  $V_1$  of the MIM's view and committed value when the honest commitment is to  $v_1$ , is indistinguishable from the joint distribution  $V_2$  when the honest commitment is to  $v_2$ . This is done as follows.

- First, we use the input-hiding property of SFE to argue that any distinguisher D that distinguishes  $V_1$  from  $V_2$ , must also distinguish these distributions when restricted to executions where  $s_1 = r_1$  in the right execution where the MIM is the committer. We prove that if this is not the case, then D can be used to guess the input  $r_1$  of the honest receiver in the right execution, contradicting the input-hiding property of SFE.
- Once this is established, we restrict ourselves to transcripts where  $s_1 = r_1$  in the right execution.
- We rely on our setting of parameters to ensure that the transcripts where  $s_1 = r_1$  in the left execution can only form a negligible fraction of all transcripts where  $s_1 = r_1$  in the right execution.
- Roughly, this means that for an overwhelming fraction of transcripts where  $s_1 = r_1$  in the right SFE execution, the left SFE execution perfectly erases the honest committer's message.
- As a result, any D that distinguishes between the distributions  $V_1$  and  $V_2$ , also distinguishes between these distributions when restricted to  $s_1 = r_1$  in the right execution. We note that conditioned on  $s_1 = r_1$ , the MIM's message can be efficiently extracted, and therefore D can be used to carefully break the (super-polynomial) hiding of the commitment Com.

This completes a sketch of our argument when  $tag_{\text{MIM}} < tag_{\mathcal{C}}$ . In case  $tag_{\text{MIM}} > tag_{\mathcal{C}}$ , this argument does not go through, since the honest committer's message is revealed with probability that is larger than the MIM's message. To deal with this situation, we append another sequential instance of SFE to our commitment, where the probability of extraction varies as a function of 2N-tag, instead of as a function of tag. This means that a committer with  $tag_{\mathcal{C}}$  will run two instances of SFE, one which transmits the committed message with probability  $\eta^{-tag_{\mathcal{C}}}$ , and another that transmits it with probability  $\eta^{2N-tag_{\mathcal{C}}}$ . Now, for  $tag_{\text{MIM}} \neq tag_{\mathcal{C}}$ , in at least one of these sessions, the probability that the MIM's message is revealed will be larger than the probability that the committer's message is revealed. Moreover, since all these probabilities of revealing messages are negligible, the other session will not reveal the committer's message except with negligible probability, and therefore, we can switch to a hybrid where the other session never outputs the committer's message. Finally, since proving security against synchronous MIM adversaries suffices for our applications, we only focus on formally proving synchronous security here, but we suspect that similar arguments would suffice to prove security of our construction against non-synchronous adversaries.

#### <span id="page-49-0"></span>7.2.1 Construction

Ingredients and notation: We will assume the existence of

• A non-interactive perfectly-binding quantum computationally hiding commitment scheme Com where there exists a constant  $c_1 > 0$  s.t. no QPT adversary has advantage better than  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda^{\operatorname{ilog}(c_1,\lambda)})$  in the hiding game.

{50}------------------------------------------------

- A two-message SFE satisfying Definition 3.8. This means that there exists a constant  $c_2 > 0$  s.t. no QPT adversary has advantage better than  $negl(\lambda^{ilog(c_2,\lambda)})$  in the quantum input privacy game.
- A quantum-secure zero-knowledge argument for NP (ZK.P, ZK.V). (We do not require multi-verifier zero-knowledge for this section.)

Let  $c = \max(c_1, c_2)$ ,  $\eta = \lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c+1,\lambda)}$ , and  $N = \mathsf{ilog}(c+1,\lambda)$ . We describe the protocol for tags or identities in [N] in Protocol 4. Also, define the language

$$\mathcal{L} = \left\{ \left( \begin{array}{c} \mathsf{c}, \mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2, \mathsf{ct}_1', \\ \mathsf{ct}_2', \mathsf{tag}, N \end{array} \right) : \exists \left( \begin{array}{c} m, r, s_1, s_2, \\ u_1, u_2 \end{array} \right) \text{ s.t. } \begin{array}{c} |s_1| = \mathsf{tag} \cdot (\log \eta), \\ |s_2| = (2N - \mathsf{tag}) \cdot (\log \eta), \\ \mathsf{ct}_1 = \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathbf{CC}[\mathsf{Id}(\cdot), s_1, (m||r)], \mathsf{ct}_1'; u_1), \\ \mathsf{ct}_2 = \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathbf{CC}[\mathsf{Id}(\cdot), s_2, (m||r)], \mathsf{ct}_2'; u_2) \end{array} \right\}.$$

<span id="page-50-1"></span>where  $Id(\cdot)$  denotes the identity function.

#### **Protocol 4**

**Common Input:**  $1^{\lambda}$  and a tag  $\in [N]$ . Set  $t_1 = \mathsf{tag} \cdot (\log \eta)$ , and  $t_2 = (2N - \mathsf{tag}) \cdot (\log \eta)$ .

C's Input: A message  $m \in \{0,1\}^{p(\lambda)}$ .

### **Commit Stage:**

- 1. C samples  $r \leftarrow U_{\lambda}$ , and sends  $c_1 = \text{Com}(1^{\lambda}, m; r)$ .
- 2. R samples  $dk_1 \leftarrow SFE.Gen(1^{\lambda}), r_1 \leftarrow U_{t_1}$ , and sends  $ct_{1,R} \leftarrow SFE.Enc_{dk_1}(r_1)$ .
- 3. C samples  $s_1 \leftarrow U_{t_1}$ ,  $u_1 \leftarrow U_{\lambda}$  and sends  $\mathsf{ct}_1 = \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Eval}\Big(\mathbf{CC}[\mathsf{Id}(\cdot), s_1, (m||r)], \mathsf{ct}_{1,\mathsf{R}}; u_1\Big)$  and  $s_1$ , where  $\mathsf{Id}(\cdot)$  is the identity function.
- 4. R samples  $dk_2 \leftarrow SFE.Gen(1^{\lambda}), r_2 \leftarrow U_{t_2}$ , and sends  $ct_{2,R} \leftarrow SFE.Enc_{dk_2}(r_2)$ .
- 5. C samples  $s_2 \leftarrow U_{t_2}$ ,  $u_2 \leftarrow U_{\lambda}$  and sends  $\mathsf{ct}_2 \leftarrow \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Eval}\Big(\mathbf{CC}[\mathsf{Id}(\cdot), s_2, (m||r)], \mathsf{ct}_{2,\mathsf{R}}; u_2\Big)$  and  $s_2$ , where  $\mathsf{Id}(\cdot)$  is the identity function.
- 6. C runs ZK.P(x, w) and R runs ZK.V(x) in an execution of ZK (with common input  $(1^{\lambda})$ ) for language  $\mathcal{L}$  (defined above), where  $x = (c_1, ct_1, ct_2, ct_{1,R}, ct_{2,R}, tag, N)$  and  $w = (m, r, s_1, s_2, u_1, u_2)$ .

Figure 4: A constant round non-malleable commitment for tags in [N], where  $N = ilog(c + 1, \lambda)$ .

#### <span id="page-50-0"></span>7.2.2 Analysis

In the reveal stage, the committer outputs (m,r) and the receiver accepts the decommitment if  $c_1 = \text{Com}(1^{\lambda}, m; r)$ . Perfect binding follows due to the perfect binding property of Com, and hiding follows by non-malleability, which we formally prove below.

**Lemma 7.1.** Protocol 4 is a non-malleable commitment according to Definition 7.1 for tags in [N].

*Proof.* It suffices to show that for every  $v_1, v_2 \in \{0,1\}^{2p(\lambda)}$  and every QPT MIM  $= \{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}$  and D  $= \{\mathsf{D}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\eta(\cdot)$  such that for large enough  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , for all  $\mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}} \in [\ell]$ 

{51}------------------------------------------------

where  $tag_{\mathcal{C}} \neq tag_{MIM}$ , the following holds.

$$\begin{split} \Big| \Pr[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda} \big( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(v_1), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \big) = 1] \\ - \Pr[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda} \big( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(v_2), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \big) = 1] \Big| = \eta(\lambda) \end{split}$$

To that end, we define the distributions

$$\begin{split} \{ \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}_{v_1,\sigma_{\lambda}} &:= \left( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(v_1), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \right) \}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}, \\ \{ \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}_{v_2,\sigma_{\lambda}} &:= \left( \mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \mathcal{C}(v_2), \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}), \sigma_{\lambda} \right) \}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}} \end{split}$$

We also define the following collections of random variables (each indexed by  $\lambda$ ). Each is defined w.r.t. a (fixed) adversary MIM =  $\{MIM_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$ , but we sometimes drop this adversary from notation for convenience. We will also sometimes condition on the MIM aborting in the left execution (where it acts as receiver). By this, we will refer to an execution where the MIM sends a message that causes an honest party to abort.

- Let  $Pr_x^{Abort}$  be the probability that MIM aborts in  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda}}$ , where  $x \in \{v_1, v_2\}$ .
- Let  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda}}^{\text{No Abort}}$  be the distribution  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda}}$  conditioned on there not being an abort.
- Let  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda}}^{\mathsf{Abort}}$  be the distribution  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda}}$  conditioned on there being an abort. Note that by definition, in this distribution, the value committed by the MIM is always set to  $\bot$ .

The following distributions will not be used explicitly in the hybrids, but will be convenient to define for the proof.

- Let  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}$  be the distribution  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda}}$  except whenever an abort occurs, the distribution outputs  $\perp$ .
- Let  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}^{\mathsf{Abort}}$  be the distribution  $\mathcal{H}_{x,\sigma_{\lambda}}$  except whenever an abort does not occur, the distribution outputs  $\perp$ .

We show that  $\{\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_{\lambda}}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}\approx_c \{\mathcal{H}_{v_2,\sigma_{\lambda}}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$  via a sequence of hybrids. In particular, we show that

$$\begin{split} \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda} &\overset{(1)}{\equiv} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_1}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda} + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_1}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda} \\ &\overset{(2)}{\approx} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_2}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda} + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_2}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda} \\ &\overset{(3)}{\approx} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_2}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}_{v_2,\sigma_\lambda} + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_2}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda} \\ &\overset{(4)}{\approx} (1 - \mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_2}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}_{v_2,\sigma_\lambda} + (\mathsf{Pr}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_2}) \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_2,\sigma_\lambda} \\ &\overset{(5)}{\equiv} \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}_{v_2,\sigma_\lambda}, \end{split}$$

where

- 1. The equalities (1) and (5) follow by definition.
- 2. The indistinguishability (2) follows as a corollary of Claim 7.1. Indeed,  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp} \approx_c \mathcal{H}_{v_2,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}$  in particular implies that the difference in the probability that the MIM aborts in both executions is negligible.
- 3. The indistinguishability (3) follows as a corollary of Claim 7.1. This can be seen by considering two cases. First, if the probability that the MIM aborts in  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda}$  is negligible, then  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda}^{\text{No Abort}} \approx_c \mathcal{H}_{v_2,\sigma_\lambda}^{\text{No Abort}}$  directly follows from Claim 7.1, and the indistinguishability follows. Otherwise, this probability is non-negligible, meaning that  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda}^{\text{Abort}}$  is efficiently sampleable. Thus, a reduction to Claim 7.1 can sample from the distribution  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda}^{\text{Abort}}$  whenever it receives  $\bot$  from its challenger. 17

<span id="page-51-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>17</sup>A more formal analysis of this can be found in [BS20, Lemma 3.2].

{52}------------------------------------------------

4. The indistinguishability (4) follows as a corollary of Claim 7.5 via a similar analysis as the last step.

#### <span id="page-52-0"></span>Claim 7.1.

$$\{\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}\approx_c\{\mathcal{H}_{v_2,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$$

*Proof.* We will prove this claim via the following sequence of hybrids. We set some notation before defining these hybrids. We will set  $t_1 = \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}} \cdot (\log \eta)$  and  $t_2 = (2N - \mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}) \cdot (\log \eta)$ . We also set  $t_1' = \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}} \cdot (\log \eta)$  and  $t_2' = (2N - \mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}) \cdot (\log \eta)$ . As a general rule, when referring to some protocol variable y in the left execution, we will use the variable as is (and denote it by y), and in the right execution, we will denote this variable by y'.

We let  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda,\perp}=\mathcal{H}_0$ .

 $\mathcal{H}_1$ : In this hybrid, the challenger executes the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimNoAbort $_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, x_{\lambda}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^*, \sigma_{\lambda}^{(x_{\lambda})})^{18}$  for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$  on  $\mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^*$ , which denotes a wrapper around the portion of the MIM that participates in Step 6 of the protocol, and an instance-advice distribution  $(x_{\lambda}, \sigma_{\lambda}^{(x_{\lambda})})$  defined as follows:

- Set the state of  $MIM_{\lambda}$  to  $\rho_{\lambda}$ .
- Execute Steps 1-5 of the protocol the same way as in the experiment  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}$ , and set  $(x,w,\mathcal{L})$  according to Protocol 4 on behalf of  $\mathcal{C}$ .
- Let  $\sigma_{\lambda}^{(x_{\lambda})}$  denote the joint distribution of the protocol transcript, the state of the MIM at the end of Step 5, and the value v' committed by the MIM in Step 1.

If there is an abort during sampling, then output  $\bot$ . Otherwise, the output of this hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{zk}$ . SimNoAbort $_\bot$ . By Lemma 6.2,

$$\mathcal{H}_0 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_1$$
.

 $\mathcal{H}_2$ : This is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except the following change.

In Step 3, C sends  $\operatorname{ct}_1 = \operatorname{SFE.Eval}\left(\operatorname{\mathbf{CC}}[\operatorname{Id}(\cdot), s_1, (0^{p(\lambda)+\lambda})], \operatorname{ct}_{1,\mathsf{R}}; u_1\right)$  and  $s_1$ . Here  $(x_\lambda, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  and  $\mathsf{V}_\lambda^*$  are defined identically to  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except with the updated  $\operatorname{ct}_1$  from Step 3, and the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimNoAbort $_\perp$   $(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  is executed. If there is an abort during sampling, then output  $\perp$ . Otherwise, the output of this hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimNoAbort $_\perp$ . We prove in Claim 7.2, that

$$\mathcal{H}_1 \approx_s \mathcal{H}_2$$
.

 $\mathcal{H}_3$ : This is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_2$  except the following change.

In Step 5, C sends  $\operatorname{ct}_2 = \operatorname{SFE.Eval}\left(\operatorname{\mathbf{CC}}[\operatorname{Id}(\cdot), s_2, (0^{p(\lambda)+\lambda})], \operatorname{ct}_{2,\mathsf{R}}; u_2\right)$  and  $s_2$ . Here  $(x_\lambda, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  and  $\mathsf{V}_\lambda^*$  are defined identically to  $\mathcal{H}_2$  except with the updated  $\operatorname{ct}_2$  from Step 5, and the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimNoAbort $_\perp$  ( $1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)}$ ) is executed. If there is an abort during sampling, then output  $\perp$ . Otherwise, the output of this hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimNoAbort $_\perp$ . We prove in Claim 7.3, that

$$\mathcal{H}_2 \approx_s \mathcal{H}_3$$
.

 $\mathcal{H}_4$ : This is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_3$  except the following change.

In Step 1, C sets  $c_1 = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda, 0; r)$ . Here  $(x_\lambda, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  and  $\mathsf{V}_\lambda^*$  are defined identically to  $\mathcal{H}_3$  except with the updated  $c_1$  from Step 1, and the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}.\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_\perp(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  is executed. If there is an abort during sampling, then output  $\bot$ . Otherwise, the output of this hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}.\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_\bot$ . We prove in Claim 7.4, that

$$\mathcal{H}_3 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4$$
.

<span id="page-52-2"></span><span id="page-52-1"></span> $<sup>^{18}</sup>$ Note that we drop the input I since there is only one verifier in this setting.

{53}------------------------------------------------

#### Claim 7.2.

$$\Delta(\mathcal{H}_1,\mathcal{H}_2) \leq 2^{-t_1} + \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_1})$$

*Proof.* Note that the output of SFE.Eval  $\left(\mathbf{CC}[\mathsf{Id}(\cdot), s_1, (m||r)], \mathsf{ct}_{1,\mathsf{R}}; u_1\right)$  is identical in both hybrids, unless  $s_1 = r_1$ . Denote by  $\mathcal{H}_1'$  the distribution that is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except it outputs  $\bot$  when  $s_1 = r_1$ . Denote by  $\mathcal{H}_2'$  the distribution that is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_2$  except it outputs  $\bot$  when  $s_1 = r_1$ . Now by statistical circuit privacy, we have that there exists a constant c > 0 such that  $\Delta(\mathcal{H}_1', \mathcal{H}_2') \le 2^{-\lambda^c}$ .

Finally, note that in each one of  $\mathcal{H}_1, \mathcal{H}_2, \mathcal{H}_1', \mathcal{H}_2'$ 

$$\Pr[s_1 = r_1] \le 2^{-t_1}.$$

Thus we have,

$$\Delta(\mathcal{H}_1, \mathcal{H}_2) \le \Delta(\mathcal{H}_1', \mathcal{H}_2') + 2 \cdot \Pr[s_1 = r_1] \le 2^{-t_1} + 2 \cdot 2^{-\lambda^c} \le 2^{-t_1} + \operatorname{negl}(2^{t_1})$$

where the last equation follows by our setting of  $t_1$ .

#### <span id="page-53-0"></span>Claim 7.3.

$$\Delta(\mathcal{H}_2, \mathcal{H}_3) \le 2^{-t_2} + \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_2})$$

*Proof.* The proof follows nearly identically to that of Claim 7.2.

#### <span id="page-53-1"></span>Claim 7.4.

$$\mathcal{H}_3 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4$$

*Proof.* Throughout this proof, we will use the notation  $\Pr[\mathsf{E}|\mathcal{H}]$  to refer to the probability that event  $\mathsf{E}$  occurs in the output of distribution  $\mathcal{H}$ .

Suppose

$$\Pr[\mathsf{MIM} \ \mathsf{aborts} | \mathcal{H}_3] = 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Then by hiding of the commitment Com

$$\Pr[\mathsf{MIM} \ \mathsf{aborts} | \mathcal{H}_4] = 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

so both hybrids output  $\bot$  except with negligible probability, and are therefore computationally indistinguishable.

Thus for the rest of this proof, we will assume that there exists a polynomial  $p(\cdot)$  such that:

$$\Pr[\mathsf{MIM} \ \mathsf{does} \ \mathsf{not} \ \mathsf{abort} \ | \mathcal{H}_3] \geq \frac{1}{p(\lambda)}.$$

By the hiding of the commitment Com,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{MIM} \ \mathsf{does} \ \mathsf{not} \ \mathsf{abort} \ | \mathcal{H}_4] \geq \frac{1}{p(\lambda)} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

For  $x \in [0,4]$ , we will denote by  $\mathcal{H}_x^{\text{No Abort}}$  the distribution  $\mathcal{H}_x$  conditioned on the MIM not aborting. Recall that whenever the MIM aborts, the two hybrids output  $\bot$ . Therefore, it suffices to prove that

$$\mathcal{H}_3^{\mathsf{No\ Abort}} \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4^{\mathsf{No\ Abort}}.$$

Now, recall that the variables  $r'_1, s'_1, t'_1$  and so on refer to the *right* execution in each experiment. For  $r'_1 \leftarrow U_{t'_1}$  and  $s'_1$  sampled independently of  $r'_1$ , we have that  $\Pr[s'_1 = r'_1] = 2^{-t'_1}$ . Then, by quantum input

{54}------------------------------------------------

privacy of SFE according to Definition 3.8, there exists a negligible function  $\mu(\cdot)$  such that for any  $x \in [0,4]$ ,  $r'_1 \leftarrow U_{t'_1}$  and  $s'_1$  chosen by the MIM in  $\mathcal{H}_x$ ,

<span id="page-54-0"></span>
$$2^{-t_1'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}) \le \Pr[s_1' = r_1' | \mathcal{H}_x^{\mathsf{No Abort}}] \le 2^{-t_1'} + \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}). \tag{6}$$

Similarly, by quantum input privacy of SFE according to Definition 3.8, there exists a negligible function  $\mu'(\cdot)$  such that for any  $x \in [0,4]$ ,  $r_2' \leftarrow U_{t_2'}$  and  $s_2'$  output by the MIM in  $\mathcal{H}_x$ ,

<span id="page-54-2"></span><span id="page-54-1"></span>
$$2^{-t_2'} - \mu'(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}) \le \Pr[s_2' = r_2' | \mathcal{H}_x^{\mathsf{No Abort}}] \le 2^{-t_2'} + \mu'(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}) \tag{7}$$

Soundness of the ZK argument, together with setting x = 0 in equations (6) and (7) implies that for  $i \in [2]$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_i'}(\mathsf{ct}_i') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_1' = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') \land (s_i' = r_i') \middle| \mathcal{H}_0^{\mathsf{No}\;\mathsf{Abort}} \right] \\ \geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left(2^{-t_i'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)})\right). \tag{8}$$

Note that for  $i \in [2]$ , SFE.Dec<sub>dk'\_i</sub>(ct'\_i)  $\to (m', r')$  s.t.  $c'_1 = \text{Com}(1^{\lambda}, m'; r') \land (s'_i = r'_i)$  can be efficiently checked by a challenger that samples  $dk'_i$  and  $r'_i$ .

Therefore, by combining Lemma 6.2 with equation (8), we have that for  $i \in [2]$ ,

<span id="page-54-3"></span>
$$\begin{aligned} &\Pr\left[\mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_i'}(\mathsf{ct}_i') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_1' = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') \wedge (s_i' = r_i') \middle| \mathcal{H}_1^{\mathsf{No}\;\mathsf{Abort}} \right] \\ &\geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left(2^{-t_i'} - \lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}\right) \\ &\geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left(2^{-t_i'} - \lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}\right) \end{aligned} \tag{9}$$

where the previous equation, for  $i \in [2]$ , follows by considering a non-uniform reduction to Claim 6.2 that fixes any transcript where SFE.  $\text{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}'_i}(\mathsf{ct}'_i) \to (m',r')$  s.t.  $\mathsf{c}'_1 = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') \land (r'_i = s'_i)$ .

Next, we split our analysis into two cases. Depending on whether  $tag_{\mathcal{C}}$  is greater or smaller than  $tag_{MIM}$ , one of the two cases will always be true.

• Case 1:  $tag_{\mathcal{C}} > tag_{\mathsf{MIM}}$ . In this case,  $t_1 = tag_{\mathcal{C}} \cdot (\log \eta)$ ,  $t_1' = tag_{\mathsf{MIM}} \cdot (\log \eta)$ . Now combining Claim 7.2 and equation (9) with i set to 1, implies:

<span id="page-54-4"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{SFE.Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_{1}'}(\mathsf{ct}_{1}') \to (m', r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_{1}' = \mathsf{Com}(1^{\lambda}, m'; r') \land (s_{1}' = r_{1}') \middle| \mathcal{H}_{2}^{\mathsf{No Abort}} \right] \\
\geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left(2^{-t_{1}'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c, \lambda)})\right) - 2^{-t_{1}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{1}}) \tag{10}$$

Next, we will carefully combine equation (10) with Claim 7.3. First, we note that the check SFE.  $\operatorname{Dec}_{\operatorname{dk}_1'}(\operatorname{ct}_1') \to (m',r')$  s.t.  $\operatorname{c}_1' = \operatorname{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') \wedge (r_1'=s_1')$  is performed before Step 5. Additionally, the only difference between  $\mathcal{H}_2^{\operatorname{No Abort}}$  and  $\mathcal{H}_3^{\operatorname{No Abort}}$  is in Step 5. As a result, for *every* (fixed) prefix of the transcript until Step 4, the distribution of Steps 5 and 6 generated according to  $\mathcal{H}_2^{\operatorname{No Abort}}$  is at most  $2^{-t_2} + \operatorname{negl}(2^{-t_2})$ -far from their distribution generated according to  $\mathcal{H}_3^{\operatorname{No Abort}}$ . This implies:

<span id="page-54-5"></span>
$$\begin{split} &\Pr\left[\mathsf{SFE.Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_{1}'}(\mathsf{ct}_{1}') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_{1}' = \mathsf{Com}(1^{\lambda},m';r') \wedge (s_{1}' = r_{1}') \middle| \boldsymbol{\mathcal{H}}_{3}^{\mathsf{No \; Abort}} \right] \\ &\geq (1 - 2^{-t_{2}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{2}})) \cdot \left( (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left( 2^{-t_{1}'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}) \right) - 2^{-t_{1}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{1}}) \right) \\ &\geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) - 2^{-t_{2}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{2}})) \cdot \left( 2^{-t_{1}'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}) \right) - 2^{-t_{1}} + 2^{-t_{1}-t_{2}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{1}}) \\ &\geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left( 2^{-t_{1}'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)}) \right) - 2^{-t_{1}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{1}}) \end{split} \tag{11}$$

{55}------------------------------------------------

Combining equation (11) with the  $negl(\lambda^{ilog(c,\lambda)})$ - hiding of the commitment Com,

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{SFE.Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_{1}'}(\mathsf{ct}_{1}') \to (m', r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_{1}' = \mathsf{Com}(1^{\lambda}, m'; r') \land (s_{1}' = r_{1}') \middle| \mathcal{H}_{4}^{\mathsf{No Abort}} \right] \\
\geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left(2^{-t_{1}'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c, \lambda)})\right) - 2^{-t_{1}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{1}}) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c, \lambda)}). \tag{12}$$

Combining equations (11), (12) with equation (6), for  $x \in [3, 4]$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_{1}'}(\mathsf{ct}_{1}') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_{1}' = \mathsf{Com}(1^{\lambda},m';r') \middle| (s_{1}' = r_{1}'), \mathcal{H}_{x}^{\mathsf{No} \, \mathsf{Abort}} \right]$$

$$\geq \frac{(1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \left(2^{-t_{1}'} - \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)})\right) - 2^{-t_{1}} - \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_{1}}) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)})}{2^{-t_{1}'} + \mu(\lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)})}$$

$$\geq 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \tag{13}$$

where the last equation follows by recalling that  $2^{-t_1'} = \eta^{-\mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}}}, 2^{-t_1} = \eta^{-\mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}}$  and  $\mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}} \geq (\mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}} + 1)$ , which implies

<span id="page-55-2"></span><span id="page-55-0"></span>
$$2^{-t_1} = \eta^{-\mathsf{tag}_{\mathcal{C}}} \le \eta^{-\mathsf{tag}_{\mathsf{MIM}} - 1} = \frac{2^{-t_1'}}{\eta}$$

for  $\eta = \lambda^{\mathsf{ilog}(c+1,\lambda)}$ .

Let us assume towards a contradiction that there exists a quantum polynomial size distinguisher D and a polynomial poly such that for large enough  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ :

<span id="page-55-1"></span>
$$\Pr[\mathsf{D} = 1 | \mathcal{H}_3^{\mathsf{No \, Abort}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{D} = 1 | \mathcal{H}_4^{\mathsf{No \, Abort}}] \ge \frac{1}{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}. \tag{14}$$

By quantum input privacy of the SFE scheme, for each  $x \in [3, 4]$ ,

$$|\Pr[\mathsf{D}=1|(s_1'\neq r_1'), \mathcal{H}_x^{\mathsf{No\ Abort}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{D}=1|(s_1'=r_1'), \mathcal{H}_x^{\mathsf{No\ Abort}}]| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

which combined with equation (14) implies that there exists a polynomial  $q(\cdot)$  such that

<span id="page-55-3"></span>
$$\Pr[\mathsf{D} = 1 | (s_1' = r_1'), \mathcal{H}_3^{\mathsf{No \, Abort}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{D} = 1 | (s_1' = r_1'), \mathcal{H}_4^{\mathsf{No \, Abort}}] \ge \frac{1}{q(\lambda)}$$
 (15)

Equations (13) and (15) together imply that:

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{D}=1 \land \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_1'}(\mathsf{ct}_1') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_1' = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') | (s_1'=r_1'), \pmb{\mathcal{H}}_3^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}] \\ &-\Pr[\mathsf{D}=1 \land \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_1'}(\mathsf{ct}_1') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_1' = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') | (s_1'=r_1'), \pmb{\mathcal{H}}_4^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}] \\ &\geq \frac{1}{q(\lambda)} \cdot (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \geq \frac{1}{2q(\lambda)} \end{split}$$

This, combined with equation (6) gives a distinguisher that distinguishes  $\mathsf{Com}(m)$  and  $\mathsf{Com}(0)$  with advantage at least  $2^{-2t_1'}$ , contradicting the hiding of  $\mathsf{Com}$  as desired.

• Case 2:  $tag_{MIM} > tag_{\mathcal{C}}$ . A similar analysis as in Case 1 implies that for any distinguisher D distinguishing  $\mathcal{H}_3^{\text{No Abort}}$  from  $\mathcal{H}_4^{\text{No Abort}}$ , there exists a polynomial  $q'(\cdot)$  such that
