$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{D}=1 \land \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_2'}(\mathsf{ct}_2') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_1' = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') | (s_2'=r_2'), \pmb{\mathcal{H}}_3^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}] \\ &-\Pr[\mathsf{D}=1 \land \mathsf{SFE}.\mathsf{Dec}_{\mathsf{dk}_2'}(\mathsf{ct}_2') \to (m',r') \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{c}_1' = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda,m';r') | (s_2'=r_2'), \pmb{\mathcal{H}}_4^{\mathsf{No \ Abort}}] \\ &\geq \frac{1}{q'(\lambda)} \end{split}$$

This, combined with equation (7) gives a distinguisher that distinguishes Com(m) and Com(0) with advantage at least  $2^{-2t_2'}$ , contradicting the hiding of Com as desired.

{56}------------------------------------------------

Claim 7.1 follows by observing that in  $\mathcal{H}_4$ , we erased all information about either one of  $v_1$  or  $v_2$ . Therefore one can perform the above hybrids in reverse order, while arguing indistinguishability, until one ends up with  $\mathcal{H}_{v_2,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}$ .

#### <span id="page-56-0"></span>Claim 7.5.

$$\{\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}^{\mathsf{Abort}}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}\approx_c\{\mathcal{H}_{v_2,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}^{\mathsf{Abort}}\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$$

*Proof.* Note that in this case, the value committed by the MIM is always  $\bot$ . Therefore, proving indistinguishability of these distributions is significantly more straightforward than in Claim 7.1. The proof again relies on a sequence of hybrid experiments, that we define below. Recall that when referring to some protocol variable y in the left execution, we will use the variable as is (and denote it by y), and in the right execution, we will denote this variable by y'.

We let 
$$\mathcal{H}^{\mathsf{Abort}}_{v_1,\sigma_\lambda,\perp} = \mathcal{H}_0.$$

 $\mathcal{H}_1$ : In this hybrid, the challenger executes one run of the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimAbort $_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, x_{\lambda}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^*, \sigma_{\lambda}^{(x_{\lambda})})^{19}$  for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$  on  $\mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^*$ , which denotes a wrapper around the portion of the MIM that participates in Step 6 of the protocol, and an instance-advice distribution  $(x_{\lambda}, \sigma_{\lambda}^{(x_{\lambda})})$  defined as follows:

- Set the state of  $MIM_{\lambda}$  to  $\rho_{\lambda}$ .
- Execute Steps 1-5 of the protocol the same way as in the experiment  $\mathcal{H}_{v_1,\sigma_{\lambda},\perp}$ , and set  $(x,w,\mathcal{L})$  according to Protocol 4 on behalf of  $\mathcal{C}$ .
- Let  $\sigma_{\lambda}^{(x_{\lambda})}$  denote the joint distribution of the protocol transcript, the state of the MIM at the end of Step 5, and the value v' committed by the MIM in Step 1.

If there is an abort during sampling then output the transcript generated until the abort happens and the state of the MIM. Otherwise, the output of the hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{zk}$ . SimAbort $_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, x_{\lambda}, \mathsf{V}_{\lambda}^{*}, \sigma_{\lambda}^{(x_{\lambda})})$ . By Lemma 6.3,

$$\mathcal{H}_0 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_1$$
.

 $\mathcal{H}_2$ : This is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except the following change.

In Step 3, C sends  $\operatorname{ct}_1 = \operatorname{SFE.Eval}\left(\operatorname{\mathbf{CC}}[\operatorname{Id}(\cdot), s_1, (0^{p(\lambda)+\lambda})], \operatorname{ct}_{1,R}; u_1\right)$  and  $s_1$ . Here  $(x_\lambda, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  and  $\mathsf{V}_\lambda^*$  are defined identically to  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except with the updated  $\operatorname{ct}_1$  from Step 3, and the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimAbort $_\perp(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  is executed. If there is an abort during sampling then output the transcript generated until the abort happens and the state of the MIM. Otherwise, the output of the hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . SimAbort $_\perp(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$ . We prove in Claim 7.6, that

$$\mathcal{H}_1 \approx_{\mathfrak{s}} \mathcal{H}_2$$
.

 $\mathcal{H}_3$ : This is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_2$  except the following change.

In Step 5, C sends  $\operatorname{ct}_2 = \operatorname{SFE.Eval}\left(\operatorname{\mathbf{CC}}[\operatorname{Id}(\cdot), s_2, (0^{p(\lambda)+\lambda})], \operatorname{ct}_{2,R}; u_2\right)$  and  $s_2$ . Here  $(x_\lambda, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  and  $\operatorname{V}_\lambda^*$  are defined identically to  $\mathcal{H}_2$  except with the updated  $\operatorname{ct}_2$  from Step 5, and the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}.\operatorname{SimAbort}_\perp(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  is executed. If there is an abort during sampling then output the transcript generated until the abort happens and the state of the MIM. Otherwise, the output of the hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}.\operatorname{SimAbort}_\perp(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$ . We prove in Claim 7.7, that

$$\mathcal{H}_2 \approx_s \mathcal{H}_3$$
.

 $\mathcal{H}_4$ : This is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_3$  except the following change.

<span id="page-56-1"></span> $<sup>^{19}</sup>$ Note that we drop the input I since there is only one verifier in this setting.

{57}------------------------------------------------

In Step 1, C sets  $c_1 = \mathsf{Com}(1^\lambda, 0; r)$ . Here  $(x_\lambda, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  and  $\mathsf{V}_\lambda^*$  are defined identically to  $\mathcal{H}_3$  except with the updated  $c_1$  from Step 1, and the simulator  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}.\mathsf{SimAbort}_\perp(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$  is executed. If there is an abort during sampling then output the transcript generated until the abort happens and the state of the MIM. Otherwise, the output of the hybrid is the output of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{zk}}.\mathsf{SimAbort}_\perp(1^\lambda, x_\lambda, \mathsf{V}_\lambda^*, \sigma_\lambda^{(x_\lambda)})$ . We prove in Claim 7.8, that

$$\mathcal{H}_3 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4$$
.

#### <span id="page-57-0"></span>Claim 7.6.

$$\Delta(\mathcal{H}_1,\mathcal{H}_2) \leq 2^{-t_1} + \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_1})$$

*Proof.* Note that the output of SFE.Eval  $\left(\mathbf{CC}[\mathsf{Id}(\cdot),s_1,(m||r)],\mathsf{ct}_{1,\mathsf{R}};u_1\right)$  is identical in both hybrids, unless  $s_1=r_1$ . Denote by  $\mathcal{H}_1'$  the distribution that is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_1$  except it outputs  $\bot$  when  $s_1=r_1$ . Denote by  $\mathcal{H}_2'$  the distribution that is identical to  $\mathcal{H}_2$  except it outputs  $\bot$  when  $s_1=r_1$ . Now by statistical circuit privacy, we have that there exists a constant c>0 such that  $\Delta(\mathcal{H}_1',\mathcal{H}_2')\leq 2^{-\lambda^c}$ .

Finally, note that in each one of  $\mathcal{H}_1, \mathcal{H}_2, \mathcal{H}_1', \mathcal{H}_2'$ ,

$$\Pr[s_1 = r_1] \le 2^{-t_1}.$$

Thus we have,

$$\Delta(\mathcal{H}_1, \mathcal{H}_2) \le \Delta(\mathcal{H}_1', \mathcal{H}_2') + 2 \cdot \Pr[s_1 = r_1] \le 2^{-t_1} + 2 \cdot 2^{-\lambda^c} \le 2^{-t_1} + \operatorname{negl}(2^{t_1})$$

where the last equation follows by our setting of  $t_1$ .

#### <span id="page-57-1"></span>Claim 7.7.

$$\Delta(\mathcal{H}_2,\mathcal{H}_3) \leq 2^{-t_2} + \mathsf{negl}(2^{t_2})$$

*Proof.* The proof follows nearly identically to that of Claim 7.2.

#### <span id="page-57-2"></span>Claim 7.8.

$$\mathcal{H}_3 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4$$

*Proof.* Recall that both hybrids only output transcripts, the value committed by the MIM, and the MIM's state when the MIM aborts at some point. Otherwise both hybrids output  $\bot$ . As such, the value committed by the MIM in every transcript in the distributions  $\mathcal{H}_3$ , and  $\mathcal{H}_4$ , is  $\bot$ .

It remains to prove that the joint distribution of the transcript and state of the MIM in  $\mathcal{H}_3$  is indistinguishable from  $\mathcal{H}_4$ , which follows immediately by the hiding of Com.

Claim 7.5 follows by observing that in  $\mathcal{H}_4$ , we erased all information about either one of  $v_1$  or  $v_2$ . Therefore one can perform the above hybrids in reverse order, while arguing indistinguishability, until one ends up with  $\mathcal{H}_{v_2,\sigma_\lambda,\perp}^{\mathsf{Abort}}$ .

This concludes the proof of the lemma.

{58}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-58-0"></span>7.3 Tag Amplification

Beginning with quantum-secure non-malleable commitments that support tags in [t], we describe how to obtain quantum-secure non-malleable commitments that support tags in  $[2^{t/2}]$ , for any  $t \leq \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ . We will apply this compiler recursively a constant number of times to our base construction to obtain a scheme for tags in  $[2^{\lambda}]$ .

The protocol itself follows nearly identically along the lines of existing tag amplification compilers in the literature [Wee10, PW10]. Each larger tag (in  $2^{t/2}$ ) is encoded into a set  $S_{\text{tag}}$  of t small tags, and this set satisfies the following property. For each pair tag, tag'  $\in 2^{t/2}$  where tag  $\neq$  tag', there exists an element in the set  $S_{\text{tag}}$  that does not lie in the set  $S_{\text{tag}}$ . Now the committer on input a message m and tag tag, generates t commitments, where the  $i^{th}$  commitment commits via the underlying non-malleable commitment to the the message m using as tag the  $i^{th}$  member of the set  $S_{\text{tag}}$ . The committer then proves to the receiver (in zero-knowledge) that all the commitments were correctly generated according to protocol specifications.

Now, the property of the tag encoding scheme guarantees that the MIM will always end up using *at least one small tag* that is different from all small tags used by the honest committer. As such, we can argue that the value committed by the MIM using this differing tag is independent of the honest committer's input.

We formally prove this via a hybrid argument, where we first rely on soundness of the ZK argument to argue that we can "focus" solely on the values committed by the MIM using the differing tag. Next, we simulate the ZK argument on behalf of the honest committer. Then we modify the values committed via all the honest small tags, while arguing that the value committed by the MIM under the differing tag does not change.

Finally, we point out an interesting feature of our amplification proof: we split the use of the ZK simulator into two cases: one simulator that only outputs non-aborting views (and otherwise outputs  $\bot$ ), and a separate one that only outputs aborting views (and otherwise outputs  $\bot$ ).

#### <span id="page-58-1"></span>7.3.1 Construction

We describe our compiler that converts a tag-based non-malleable commitment scheme for tags in  $t(\lambda)$ , where  $t(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , into one that supports tags in  $2^{t/2}$ , while adding only a constant number of rounds. This is formalized in Protocol 5. We will let nmCom denote a non-malleable commitment for tags in  $t(\lambda)$ , and we denote the message length by  $p(\lambda)$ .

**Ingredients and notation:** We will assume the existence of a quantum-secure zero-knowledge argument for NP (ZK.P, ZK.V). (We do not require multi-verifier zero-knowledge for this section.)

#### <span id="page-58-2"></span>7.3.2 Analysis.

In the reveal stage, the committer outputs  $(m, r_{1,C})$  and the receiver accepts the decommitment if this produces is a valid decommitment of  $c_1$  according to nmCom. The perfect binding property of the scheme in Protocol 5 follows directly from the perfect binding property of the underlying protocol nmCom. Hiding follows from non-malleability, the proof of which is in Appendix C.

<span id="page-58-4"></span>**Lemma 7.2.** Protocol 5 is a one-one non-malleable commitment according to Definition 7.1 for tags in  $[2^{t/2}]$ .

We also have the following lemma, that follows by a standard hybrid argument, due to [LPV08].

<span id="page-58-3"></span>**Lemma 7.3.** Every quantum-secure non-malleable commitment satisfying Definition 7.1 also satisfies Definition 7.2.

We conclude this section with the following theorem, that can be obtained by applying the compiler in Protocol 5 (c+1) times to the base non-malleable commitment from Protocol 4, and then applying Lemma 7.3.

**Theorem 7.1.** Assuming there exists a constant  $c \in \mathbb{N}$  such that at all quantum polynomial size circuits have advantage  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda^{\operatorname{ilog}(c,\lambda)})$  in distinguishing LWE samples from uniform, and spooky encryption for relations computable by quantum circuits, there exist quantum-secure constant round non-malleable commitments satisfying Definition 7.2.

{59}------------------------------------------------

#### **Protocol 5**

<span id="page-59-1"></span>**Common Input:** Security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$ , tag  $\in [2^{t/2}]$  represented as  $\{tag_i\}_{i\in[t/2]}$ . Here  $tag_i = i||tag[i]|$  where tag[i] denotes the  $i^{th}$  bit of tag.

C's Input: A string  $m \in \{0,1\}^{p(\lambda)}$ .

### **Commit Stage:**

- Stage 1: In parallel, for all  $i \in [k]$ , C runs nmCom.C $(m; r_{i,C})$  and R runs R $(r_{i,R})$  with common input tag $_i$ . Let  $c_i$  and  $\phi_i$  denote the set of all messages generated by C and R respectively in the  $i^{th}$  parallel execution.
- Stage 2: C executes  $\mathsf{ZK.P}(x,w,\mathcal{L})$  and R executes  $\mathsf{ZK.V}(x,\mathcal{L})$  where:
  - $x = \{c_i\}_{i \in [t/2]}, w = (m, \{r_{i,C}\}_{i \in [t/2]}),$
  - $\mathcal{L} = \left\{ \{b_i\}_{i \in [t/2]} \middle| \exists \left(a, \ \{s_i\}_{i \in [t/2]}\right) \text{ s.t. } \forall i \in [t/2], \mathsf{nmCom.C}(a, \mathsf{tag}_i, \phi_i; s_i) = b_i \right\}$
  - where nmCom.C(a, tag $_i$ ,  $\phi_i$ ;  $s_i$ ) denotes the transcript output by C $_i$  on input receiver messages  $\phi_i$ .

Figure 5: A constant round non-malleable commitment for tags in  $[2^{t/2}]$ .

Finally, we remark that a folklore technique [DDN91] where the committer and receiver participate in rounds, sending  $\bot$  in every round, except for the committer sending  $\mathsf{ECom}(m;r)$  in round i (where  $i=\mathsf{tag}$ ) using a single-committer extractable commitment yields a one-to-one non-malleable commitment for  $\mathsf{tag} \in [N]$  in the synchronous setting, in O(N) rounds, for any  $N \le \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ . Setting  $N = \mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)$  for any constant  $c \in \mathbb{N}$  yields a protocol with  $O(\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda))$  rounds for  $\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda)$  tags. Applying our tag amplification compiler to this scheme (c+1) times, yields a non-malleable commitment for tags in  $2^\lambda$  against synchronous adversaries, in  $O(\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda))$  rounds. The underlying extractable commitment can be instantiated using the technique of [BS20] based on polynomial quantum hardness of LWE and polynomial hardness of QFHE. This yields  $O(\mathsf{ilog}(c,\lambda))$  round post-quantum non-malleable commitments from polynomial hardness assumptions.

### <span id="page-59-0"></span>8 Quantum-Secure Multi-Party Coin-Flipping

We now combine the primitives constructed in earlier sections to build a constant-round coin-flipping protocol secure against quantum polynomial-time adversaries. The protocol was described at a high level in Section 2.5, and is given in full detail in Protocol 6. As explained in the overview, each party will first commit to random strings  $c_i$ ,  $r_i$  using a non-malleable commitment, then commit to  $c_i$  using our parallel extractable commitment with randomness  $r_i$ , and finally broadcast  $c_i$ . The parties will output  $\bigoplus_{i \in [n]} c_i$  as the common output if all parties manage to prove in zero-knowledge that they behaved honestly throughout the protocol.

**Proof Strategy.** Our simulator will be structurally similar to the zero-knowledge simulator described in Section 6, in the sense that we build a simulator  $\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_\perp$  specifically for non-aborting transcripts and a simulator  $\mathsf{SimAbort}_\perp$  specifically for aborting transcripts. The bulk of the work in  $\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_\perp$  involves sampling instances and an advice state (consisting of the adversary's view through Step 5 of the protocol) for the final part of the adversary, which in particular interacts with honest parties in order to verify their zero-knowledge arguments in Step 6. These arguments are then simulated by (part of) the zero-knowledge simulator  $\mathsf{ZK}.\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_\perp$ , which takes as input the adversary and the sampled advice state.

{60}------------------------------------------------

However, as alluded to in Section 2.5, once this simulation is performed in the hybrids, it is no longer possible to directly invoke the soundness of the adversary's zero-knowledge arguments in Step 6, when changing how  $\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}$  samples the advice state. Thus, we invoke soundness in the very first hybrid to claim that the following "check" never fails, except with negligible probability.

The check fails if the Step 1-5 messages sent by at least one of the malicious parties are not explainable, yet the honest parties do not abort. If this check fails, we simply append check-fail to the transcript. Now, this check will continue to be computed in later hybrids, but we can claim that since (as we show) all later hybrids are indistinguishable from the first hybrid, check-fail must also only appear with negligible probability in these later hybrids. When it comes time to invoke the non-malleability of the honest party commitments, we can use the fact that check-fail appears with negligible probability to show that a malicious party cannot even change its *extractable* commitment based on the changing simulated view. If it could, then since non-malleability implies that its previously sent non-malleable commitment couldn't change, then it must be the case that its messages are no longer explainable (since its two commitments are no longer consistent). Thus, the check will fail and appear in the hybrid's output, a contradiction. Of course, turning this intuition into a formal proof requires much care, especially since this check is inefficient. Thus, we will make liberal use of non-uniform fixing arguments.

We also remark that it would be most natural to rely on *many-to-many* non-malleable commitments in this multi-party setting. However, we only have a post-quantum construction of one-one commitments in Section 7. Thus, when invoking non-malleability, a reduction must isolate the commitment of a single malicious party that would constitute a mauling attack. We again use both non-uniformity and the check-fail condition here, showing that any mauling attack would cause check-fail to appear, and thus that there must exist *some* malicious party for which the check fails over specifically its messages. The identity of this party can then be given as non-uniform advice to a reduction.

### <span id="page-60-0"></span>8.1 Definition

<span id="page-60-2"></span>**Definition 8.1** (Quantum-Secure Fully-Simulatable Multi-Party Coin-Flipping). Let  $k = k(\lambda)$  be any fixed polynomial. An fully-simulatable n-party k-coin-flipping protocol with quantum security is given by n classical interactive Turing machines  $(P_1, \ldots, P_n)$  with joint input  $(1^{\lambda}, 1^n)$  and outputs  $r_i \in \{0, 1\}^{k(\lambda)} \cup \{\bot\}$ .

Given a coin-flipping protocol and an adversary  $A^* = \{A_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  that corrupts a set of parties  $\mathbb{S} \subset [n]$ , let  $\mathbb{H}$  denote  $[n] \setminus \mathbb{S}$  and define the random variable  $\text{Real}(A_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda})$  to consist of the outputs of honest parties  $\{P_i\}_{i \in \mathbb{H}}$  as well as the view  $\text{VIEW}_{A_{\lambda}^*} \langle A_{\lambda}^*(\rho_{\lambda}), \{P_i\}_{i \in \mathbb{H}} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, 1^n)$  of  $A_{\lambda}^*$  after executing the protocol in the presence of  $A_{\lambda}^*$ .

We require the following security property. Fix any  $\mathbb{S} \subset [n]$ . There exists a quantum expected polynomial-time simulator  $\mathsf{Sim}$ , such that for any quantum polynomial-size adversary  $\mathsf{A}^* = \{\mathsf{A}^*_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  that participates in the protocol, generating joint messages on behalf of all algorithms in  $\mathbb{S}$ ,

$$\{ \mathsf{Real}(\mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}) \}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}} \approx_c \{ \mathsf{Sim}(1^{\lambda}, 1^n, r, \mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}) \mid r \leftarrow U_{k(\lambda)} \}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}, \tag{16}$$

where for the "protocol output" part of its simulation output,  $Sim(1^{\lambda}, 1^n, r, A_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda})$  is restricted to output either  $r^{|\mathbb{H}|}$  or  $\perp^{|\mathbb{H}|}$ .

#### <span id="page-60-1"></span>8.2 Construction

**Ingredients**: All of the following are assumed to be quantum-secure.

- A many-to-one non-malleable commitment nmCom = (nmCom.C, nmCom.R).
- A multi-committer extractable commitment eCom = (eCom.C, eCom.R).
- A multi-verifier publicly-verifiable zero-knowledge argument for NP ZK = (ZK.P, ZK.V).

**Languages.** We define two NP languages  $\mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{eCom})}$  and  $\mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{both})}$ . Let  $(x,y) \coloneqq \langle \mathsf{nmCom.C}(c;r), \mathsf{nmCom.R}_y \rangle$  denote the transcript of an execution of nmCom where the receiver messages are fixed to y and x is the set

{61}------------------------------------------------

of resulting committer messages. Similarly, let  $(x,y) \coloneqq \langle \mathsf{eCom.C}(c;r), \mathsf{eCom.R}_y \rangle$  denote the transcript of an execution between some eCom.C and eCom.R (which may be part of a larger eCom transcript involving other committers) where the receiver messages are fixed to y and x is the set of resulting committer messages. Then  $\mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{eCom})}$  and  $\mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{both})}$  are defined as follows.

$$\begin{split} \mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{eCom})} &\coloneqq \left\{ (x,y) \ \middle| \ \exists (c,r) \ \mathsf{s.t.} \ (x,y) \coloneqq \langle \mathsf{eCom.C}(c;r), \mathsf{eCom.R}_y \rangle \right\} \\ \mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{both})} &\coloneqq \left\{ (x,y,x',y',c) \ \middle| \ \exists (r,s) \ \mathsf{s.t.} \ \begin{array}{l} (x,y) \coloneqq \langle \mathsf{nmCom.C}((c,r);s), \mathsf{nmCom.R}_y \rangle, \\ (x',y') \coloneqq \langle \mathsf{eCom.C}(c;r), \mathsf{eCom.R}_{y'} \rangle \end{array} \right\} \end{split}$$

#### **Protocol 6**

<span id="page-61-1"></span>**Common input**: Security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$  and number of parties  $1^{n}$ .

- 1. For all  $i \in [n]$ ,  $P_i$  samples  $c_i \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{k(\lambda)}, \{r_{i,j}, s_{i,j}\}_{j \in [n] \setminus \{i\}} \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{2(n-1)\lambda}$ .
- 2. For all  $i \in [n], j \in [n] \setminus \{i\}$ ,  $P_i$  runs nmCom.C $((c_i, r_{i,j}); s_{i,j})$  with tag i and  $P_j$  runs nmCom.R to produce

 $\left(\alpha_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{c})},\alpha_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{r})}\right) \leftarrow \langle \mathsf{nmCom.C}((c_i,r_{i,j});s_{i,j}), \mathsf{nmCom.R}\rangle(1^\lambda),$ 

where  $\alpha_{i,j}^{(c)}$  denotes the committer messages sent by  $P_i$  and  $\alpha_{i,j}^{(r)}$  denotes the receiver messages sent by  $P_i$ .

3. For all  $j \in [n]$ ,  $P_j$  runs eCom.R and each  $P_i$  for  $i \neq j$  runs eCom. $C_i(c_i; r_{i,j})$  to produce

$$\left(\left\{\beta_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}\right\}_{i\in[n]\backslash\{j\}},\left\{\beta_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{r})}\right\}_{i\in[n]\backslash\{j\}}\right)\leftarrow\langle\{\mathsf{eCom.C}_i(c_i;r_{i,j})\}_{i\in[n]\backslash\{j\}},\mathsf{eCom.R}\rangle(1^\lambda,1^{n-1})$$

where  $\beta_{i,j}^{(c)}$  denotes the committer messages sent by  $P_i$  and  $\beta_{i,j}^{(r)}$  denotes the receiver messages sent by  $P_j$ .

- 4. For all  $i \in [n]$ ,  $P_i$  runs  $\mathsf{ZK.P}(\{x_{i,j}, w_{i,j}\}_{j \in [n] \setminus \{i\}})$  and each  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [n] \setminus \{i\}}$  runs  $\mathsf{ZK.V}_j(x_{i,j})$  in an execution of  $\mathsf{ZK}$  with common input  $(1^\lambda, 1^{n-1})$  for language  $\mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{eCom})}$  (defined above), where  $x_{i,j} = (\beta_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}, \beta_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{r})})$  and  $w_{i,j} = (c_i, r_{i,j})$ .
- 5. For all  $i \in [n]$ ,  $P_i$  broadcasts  $c_i$ .
- 6. For all  $i \in [n]$ ,  $P_i$  runs  $\mathsf{ZK.P}(\{x_{i,j}, w_{i,j}\}_{j \in [n] \setminus \{i\}})$  and each  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [n] \setminus \{i\}}$  runs  $\mathsf{ZK.V}_j(x_{i,j})$  in an execution of  $\mathsf{ZK}$  with common input  $(1^\lambda, 1^{n-1})$  for language  $\mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{both})}$  (defined above), where  $x_{i,j} = (\alpha_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}, \alpha_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{r})}, \beta_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}, \beta_{i,j}^{(\mathsf{r})}, c_i)$  and  $w_{i,j} = (r_{i,j}, s_{i,j})$ .
- 7. For all  $i \in [n]$ ,  $P_i$  runs the ZK verification algorithm on all 2n(n-1) proofs provided in Steps 4 and 6. If all proofs are accepting,  $P_i$  outputs  $\bigoplus_{i \in [n]} c_i$ , and otherwise outputs  $\bot$ .

Figure 6: A quantum-secure constant-round coin-flipping protocol.

### <span id="page-61-0"></span>8.3 Security

**Theorem 8.1.** For any n and polynomial  $k(\lambda)$ , Protocol 6 is a quantum-secure fully-simulatable n-party k-coin-flipping protocol.

{62}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* Fix a number of parties n, a polynomial  $k=k(\lambda)$ , and a set  $\mathbb{S}\subset [n]$  of malicious parties. We construct a simulator Sim that for every quantum polynomial-size adversary  $\mathsf{A}^*=\{\mathsf{A}_\lambda^*,\rho_\lambda\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}$  corrupting parties in  $\mathbb{S}$ , outputs a distribution

 $\{\mathsf{Sim}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, r, \mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^{*}, \rho_{\lambda}) \mid r \leftarrow U_{k(\lambda)}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ 

that satisfies the conditions of Definition 8.1. Similar to the proof of zero-knowledge in Section 6.4, the simulator Sim will make use of two sub-routines,  $SimNoAbort_{\perp}$  and  $SimAbort_{\perp}$ .

 $\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, r, \mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^{*}, \rho_{\lambda})$ :

1. Let  $i^*$  denote the smallest index of a party in  $\mathbb{H}$ . Define the machine  $V_{\lambda}^*$  as follows.  $V_{\lambda}^*$  will act on behalf of verifiers  $\{\mathsf{ZK}.\mathsf{V}_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$  in the ZK session in Step 6 of Protocol 6 where party  $P_{i^*}$  is the prover. Thus, it consists of the portion of  $\mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^*$  that interacts during this step *as well as* the portion of the honest parties  $\mathbb{H}$  that interact in the n-1 sessions where  $P_{i^*}$  is *not* the prover.

We will next describe how a particular instance-advice distribution  $(\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}},\sigma^{\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}})$  is generated for  $\mathsf{V}^*_\lambda$ . Generating this distribution will involve simulating Steps 1-5 of Protocol 6 for adversary  $\mathsf{A}^*_\lambda$ . In particular, the advice state  $\sigma^{\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}}$  will include the transcript  $\tau^{(5)}$  of the entire simulated execution through Step 5, the inner state  $\rho^{(5)}$  of  $\mathsf{A}^*_\lambda$  at this point, as well as the witnesses  $\{r_{i,j},s_{i,j}\}_{i\in\mathbb{H}\setminus\{i^*\},j\in[n]\setminus\{i\}}$  to be used by parties  $\{P_i\}_{i\in\mathbb{H}\setminus\{i^*\}}$  in Step 6 of Protocol 6. The instances  $\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$  will be a subset of  $\tau^{(5)}$ . In particular, for each  $j\in\mathbb{S}$ ,  $x_j$  will be set to  $(\alpha^{(c)}_{i^*,j},\alpha^{(r)}_{i^*,j},\beta^{(c)}_{i^*,j},\beta^{(r)}_{i^*,j},c'_{i^*})$ , which are the messages exchanged by  $i^*$  and j during Steps 2 and 3 when  $P_{i^*}$  was acting as a committer, as well the value  $c'_{i^*}$  broadcast by  $P_{i^*}$  in Step 5.

This instance-advice distribution is generated as follows.

- (a) For each party  $\{P_i\}_{i\in\mathbb{H}\setminus\{i^*\}}$ , sample  $c_i,\{r_{i,j},s_{i,j}\}_{j\in[n]\setminus\{i\}}$  as in Step 1 of Protocol 6.
- (b) Set  $\rho^{(1)} := \rho_{\lambda}$  to be the inner state of  $A_{\lambda}^*$ , and interact with  $A_{\lambda}^*$  to run Step 2 of Protocol 6 honestly, with the only difference being that party  $P_{i^*}$  commits to  $(0^{k(\lambda)}, 0^{\lambda})$ . Let  $\rho^{(2)}$  be the resulting inner state of  $A_{\lambda}^*$ , and let  $\{\alpha_{i^*,j}^{(c)}, \alpha_{i^*,j}^{(r)}\}_{j \in \mathbb{S}}$  be the messages sent between  $P_{i^*}$  and  $\mathbb{S}$  in commitments where  $P_{i^*}$  was the committer.
- (c) Define the machine  $\mathsf{C}^*_\lambda$  as follows.  $\mathsf{C}^*_\lambda$  will act on behalf of committers  $\{\mathsf{eCom}.\mathsf{C}_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$  in the eCom session in Step 3 of Protocol 6 where party  $P_{i^*}$  is the receiver. Thus, it consists of the portion of  $\mathsf{A}^*_\lambda$  that interacts during this step as well as the portion of the honest parties  $\mathbb{H}$  that interact in the n-1 sessions where  $P_{i^*}$  is not the receiver. The advice state  $\sigma_\lambda$  given to  $\mathsf{C}^*_\lambda$  will include the transcript  $\tau^{(2)}$  of the execution so far, the inner state  $\rho^{(2)}$  of  $\mathsf{A}^*_\lambda$  at this point, and the messages and randomness  $\{c_i, r_{i,j}\}_{i\in\mathbb{H}, j\in[n]\setminus\{i\}}$  to be used in the commitments by honest players in Step 3, where  $c_{i^*}=0^{k(\lambda)}$  and  $r_{i^*,j}$  is uniformly and independently sampled from the rest of the transcript. The view of  $\mathsf{C}^*_\lambda$  at the end of this interaction includes the updated execution transcript  $\tau^{(3)}$  as well as the updated inner state  $\rho^{(3)}$  of  $\mathsf{A}^*_\lambda$ .

Now, compute

$$(\{\tau_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}},\mathsf{st},\{c_j'\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}})\leftarrow\mathsf{eCom}.\mathsf{Ext}(1^\lambda,1^{n-1},\mathbb{S},\mathsf{C}_\lambda^*,\sigma_\lambda),$$

and parse  $(\{\tau_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}, \mathsf{st})$  to obtain  $\tau^{(3)}$  and  $\rho^{(3)}$ , where  $\tau^{(3)}$  in particular includes the messages  $\{\beta_{i^*,j}^{(\mathsf{c})},\beta_{i^*,j}^{(\mathsf{r})}\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$  exchanged by  $P_{i^*}$  and  $\mathbb{S}$  in commitments where some party  $j\in\mathbb{S}$  was the receiver. If eCom.Ext produced an abort transcript, then return  $\bot$ , and otherwise continue, setting  $\rho^{(3)}$  to be the state of  $\mathsf{A}^*_\lambda$ .

- (d) Interact with  $A_{\lambda}^*$  to run Steps 4 and 5 of the protocol honestly, with the only difference being that party  $P_{i^*}$  broadcasts  $c'_{i^*} \coloneqq \bigoplus_{j \in \mathbb{S}} c'_j \bigoplus_{j \in \mathbb{H} \setminus \{i^*\}} c_j \oplus r$  in Step 5.
- (e) If there exists  $j \in \mathbb{S}$  such that the output  $c_j$  of  $P_j$  in Step 5 is not equal to  $c_j'$ , then return  $\bot$ . Otherwise, let  $\tau^{(5)}$  denote the transcript so far and let  $\rho^{(5)}$  denote the state of  $\mathsf{A}^*_\lambda$  at the end of Step 5. For each  $j \in \mathbb{S}$  set  $x_j = (\alpha_{i^*,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}, \alpha_{i^*,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}, \beta_{i^*,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}, \beta_{i^*,j}^{(\mathsf{c})}, c_{i^*}')$ , and set  $\sigma^{\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}} = (\tau^{(5)}, \rho^{(5)}, \{r_{i,j}, s_{i,j}\}_{i\in\mathbb{H}\setminus\{i^*\},j\in[n]\setminus\{i\}})$ .

{63}------------------------------------------------

### 2. Now, compute

$$\mathsf{VIEW} \leftarrow \mathsf{ZK}.\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n-1}, \mathbb{S}, \{x_j\}_{j \in \mathbb{S}}, \mathsf{V}^*_{\lambda}, \sigma^{\{x_j\}_{j \in \mathbb{S}}}).$$

If the proofs (which are included in VIEW) output by  $A_{\lambda}^*$  are accepting, then output VIEW, and otherwise output  $\perp$ .

SimAbort<sub> $\perp$ </sub>(1<sup> $\lambda$ </sup>, 1<sup>n</sup>, r, A<sup>\*</sup><sub> $\lambda$ </sub>,  $\rho_{\lambda}$ ):

1. Set  $\rho_{\lambda}$  as the initial state of  $A_{\lambda}^*$ . Execute Steps 1-6 with  $A_{\lambda}^*$  using honest party strategy according to Protocol 6 on behalf of parties  $\{P_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{H}}$ . If sampling this distribution leads to an abort at any point, halt and return the view of  $A_{\lambda}^*$ . Otherwise, return  $\bot$ .

 $\mathsf{SimComb}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \mathsf{A}^{*}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda})$ : With probability 1/2, execute  $\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \{x_{i}\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{V}^{*}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda})$  and otherwise execute  $\mathsf{SimAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n}, I, \mathsf{A}^{*}_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda})$ .

 $\mathsf{Sim}(1^\lambda, 1^n, I, \mathsf{A}^*_\lambda, \rho_\lambda)$ : Let  $\overline{\mathsf{SimComb}}_\bot(\cdot) \coloneqq \mathsf{SimComb}_\bot(1^\lambda, 1^n, I, \mathsf{A}^*_\lambda, \cdot)$  be the circuit  $\mathsf{SimComb}_\bot$  with all inputs hard-coded except for  $\rho_\lambda$ , and output  $\mathsf{R}(\overline{\mathsf{SimComb}}_\bot, \rho_\lambda, \lambda)$ , where  $\mathsf{R}$  is the algorithm from Lemma 3.1.

This concludes the description of the simulator. Before proceeding to the proof of indistinguishability, we define the following collections of random variables (each indexed by  $\lambda$ ). Each is defined with respect to the adversary  $A^* = \{A_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  that we are considering. Throughout, whenever we say abort, we mean that either one of the parties controlled by the adversary aborts, or it fails to prove one of its statements.

- Let  $\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(\mathsf{A}^*) \coloneqq \{\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^n, r, \mathsf{A}^*_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda})\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}.$
- Let  $\mathsf{SimAbort}_{\perp}(\mathsf{A}^*) \coloneqq \{\mathsf{SimAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^n, r, \mathsf{A}^*_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda})\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}.$
- Let  $\mathsf{SimComb}_{\perp}(\mathsf{A}^*) \coloneqq \{\mathsf{SimComb}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^n, r, \mathsf{A}^*_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda})\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}.$
- Let  $Sim(V^*) := \{Sim(1^{\lambda}, 1^n, r, A_{\lambda}^*, \rho_{\lambda})\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}.$
- Let RealNoAbort $_{\perp}(A^*)$  be the distribution Real $(A^*)$ , except that whenever an abort occurs, the distribution outputs  $\perp$ .
- Let RealAbort $_{\perp}(A^*)$  be the distribution Real $(A^*)$ , except that if an abort *does not* occur, the distribution outputs  $\perp$ .
- Let  $\mathsf{SimComb}(\mathsf{A}^*)$  be the distribution  $\mathsf{SimComb}_\perp$  conditioned on the output not being  $\perp$ .

Next, we prove the following claim.

#### Claim 8.1.

$$\mathsf{RealNoAbort}_{\perp}(\mathsf{A}^*) \approx_c \mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(\mathsf{A}^*)$$

*Proof.* This can be proved via the following sequence of hybrids.

- $\mathcal{H}_0$ : RealNoAbort $_{\perp}(A^*)$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_1$ : This hybrid is the same as  $\mathcal{H}_0$ , except that it attaches check-fail to the output if the following (inefficient check) on Steps 1-4 of the transcript, fails.
  - Let  $i^*$  denote the smallest index in  $\mathbb{H}$ . For  $j \in \mathbb{S}$ , let  $y_j \coloneqq (\alpha_{j,i^*}^{(c)}, \alpha_{j,i^*}^{(r)}, \beta_{j,i^*}^{(c)}, \beta_{j,i^*}^{(r)}, c_j)$  be the messages exchanged between  $P_{i^*}$  and  $P_j$  in Steps 2 and 3 when  $P_j$  was acting as the committer, along with the message broadcast by  $P_j$  in Step 4. The check fails if there exists a  $j \in \mathbb{S}$  such that  $y_j \notin \mathcal{L}^{(both)}$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_2$ : Let  $V_{\lambda}^*$  be the machine defined in the description of SimNoAbort $_{\perp}$ . Sample instance-advice distribution  $(\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}, \sigma^{\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}})$  as described below.

{64}------------------------------------------------

- 1. Execute Steps 1-4 of the protocol identically to  $\mathcal{H}_2$ . Let  $\tau^{(4)}$  denote the transcript generated so far, let  $\rho^{(4)}$  denote the state of  $A_{\lambda}^*$  at the end of Step 4, and let  $\{r_{i,j}, s_{i,j}\}_{i \in \mathbb{H} \setminus \{i^*\}, j \in [n] \setminus \{i\}}$  be strings drawn in Step 1 of the protocol.
- 2. If the check described in  $\mathcal{H}_2$  fails, then attach check-fail to the transcript.
- 3. For  $j \in \mathbb{S}$ , let  $x_j := (\alpha_{i^*,j}^{(c)}, \alpha_{i^*,j}^{(r)}, \beta_{i^*,j}^{(c)}, \beta_{i^*,j}^{(r)}, c_{i^*}')$  be the messages exchanged between  $P_{i^*}$  and  $P_j$  in Steps 2 and 3 when  $P_{i^*}$  was acting as the committer and  $P_j$  was acting as the receiver, along with the message broadcast by  $P_{i^*}$  in Step 4. Set  $\sigma^{\{x_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}} = (\tau^{(4)}, \rho^{(4)}, \{r_{i,j}, s_{i,j}\}_{i\in\mathbb{H}\setminus\{i^*\},j\in[n]\setminus\{i\}})$ .

Now, compute

$$\mathsf{VIEW} \leftarrow \mathsf{ZK}.\mathsf{SimNoAbort}_{\perp}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n-1}, \mathbb{S}, \mathsf{V}^*_{\lambda}, (\{x_j\}_{j \in \mathbb{S}}, \sigma^{\{x_j\}_{j \in \mathbb{S}}})).$$

If the proofs (which are included in VIEW) output by  $A_{\lambda}^*$  are accepting, then output VIEW, and otherwise output  $\perp$ .

- $\mathcal{H}_3$ : Sample instance-advice distribution identically to  $\mathcal{H}_2$ , except that in Step 2, party  $P_{i^*}$  commits to  $(0^{k(\lambda)}, 0^{\lambda})$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_4$ : Sample instance-advice distribution identically to  $\mathcal{H}_3$ , except that in Step 4, the ZK session where  $P_{i^*}$  is the prover is simulated.
- $\mathcal{H}_5$ : Sample instance-advice distribution identically to  $\mathcal{H}_4$ , except that in Step 3, for all  $j \in [n] \setminus \{i^*\}$ , party  $P_{i^*}$  commits to  $0^{k(\lambda)}$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_6$ : Sample instance-advice distribution identically to  $\mathcal{H}_5$ , except that in Step 4, the ZK session where  $P_{i^*}$  is the prover is performed honestly, with witnesses  $\{(0^{k(\lambda)}, r_{i^*,j})\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_7$ : Let  $C^* = (C_{\lambda}^*, \sigma_{\lambda})_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  be the machine and corresponding non-uniform advice as defined in the description of SimNoAbort $_{\perp}$ . Sample instance-advice distribution identically to  $\mathcal{H}_6$ , except that the values committed by  $A^*$  in interaction with  $P_{i^*}$  are extracted as in the description of Sim. Additionally, this hybrid outputs  $\bot$  if the values  $\{c_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$  output by parties  $\{P_j\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$  in Step 5 do not match the extracted  $\{c_j'\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}}$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_8$ : This hybrid is the distribution SimNoAbort $_{\perp}(A^*)$ . The only differences between  $\mathcal{H}_7$  and  $\mathcal{H}_8$  are the check introduced in  $\mathcal{H}_1$  is removed, and:
  - In  $\mathcal{H}_7$ , the challenger generates  $P_{i^*}$ 's message in Step 4 by sampling uniformly random  $c_{i^*}$ .
  - In  $\mathcal{H}_8$ , the challenger generates  $P_{i^*}$ 's message in Step 4 as  $c_{i^*} = \bigoplus_{j \in \mathbb{S}} c_j' \bigoplus_{j \in \mathbb{H} \setminus \{i^*\}} c_j \oplus r$ .

Now we show that each consecutive pair of hybrids is indistinguishable. We let  $BAD_i$  be the event that in hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_i$ , check-fail appears in the output distribution (meaning that the check introduced in  $\mathcal{H}_1$  failed AND there was no abort).

- $\mathcal{H}_0 \approx_s \mathcal{H}_1$ : It suffices to show that  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_1] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , which follows directly from the quantum computational soundness of ZK.
- $\mathcal{H}_1 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_2$ : This follows from Lemma 6.2.
- $\mathcal{H}_2 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_3$ : We define distributions  $\mathcal{H}_2'$  and  $\mathcal{H}_3'$  that are identical to  $\mathcal{H}_2$  and  $\mathcal{H}_3$  respectively, except that  $\mathcal{H}_2'$  and  $\mathcal{H}_3'$  do not perform the additional check described in  $\mathcal{H}_1$ , and as such, never attach check-fail to the output.

To prove that  $\mathcal{H}_2$  and  $\mathcal{H}_3$  are computationally indistinguishable, it suffices to prove that  $\mathcal{H}_2 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_2'$ ,  $\mathcal{H}_2' \approx_c \mathcal{H}_3'$ , and  $\mathcal{H}_3' \approx_c \mathcal{H}_3$ . The first indistinguishability follows from the fact that  $\mathcal{H}_2 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_1$  and  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_1] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , which means that  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_2] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . The second indistinguishability

{65}------------------------------------------------

follows directly from the hiding of nmCom (implied by Definition 7.1). In what follows, we show that  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_3] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , which implies that  $\mathcal{H}_3' \approx_c \mathcal{H}_3$ .

Let  $\mathsf{BAD}_{3,j}$  be the event that, in hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_3$ ,  $y_j \notin \mathcal{L}^{(\mathsf{both})}$  (where  $y_j$  was defined in  $\mathcal{H}_1$ ) and yet the hybrid did not abort. Now suppose that there exists a polynomial  $p(\cdot)$  such that for large enough  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_3] \geq 1/p(\lambda)$ . Assuming that  $n = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , this implies that there exists a polynomial  $p'(\cdot)$  such that for large enough  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , there exists some  $j_{\lambda}^*$  such that  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_{3,j^*}] \geq 1/p'(\lambda)$ .

We will use this to contradict many-to-one non-malleability of nmCom, by building a QPT man-in-the-middle adversary MIM =  $\{MIM_{\lambda}, \sigma_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  that uses A\* to contradict Definition 7.1.

 $\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}$  obtains as non-uniform advice i) the index  $j_{\lambda}^*$  that maximizes  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_{3,j^*}]$  and ii)  $\mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^*$ 's advice state  $\rho_{\lambda}$ . It simulates the first two steps of the coin-flipping protocol in the presence of  $\mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^*$ . During Step 2, it interacts with a challenger on the left committing to either  $(c_{i^*}, r_{i^*,j})$  for each  $j \in \mathbb{S}$ , or to  $(0^{k(\lambda)}, 0^{\lambda})$  for each  $j \in \mathbb{S}$ , on behalf of  $P_{i^*}$ . It forwards these to  $\mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^*$  on behalf of  $P_{i^*}$  and uses the strategy in  $\mathcal{H}_2$  to generate messages on behalf of all other honest parties. When  $\mathsf{A}_{\lambda}^*$  outputs committer messages computed on behalf of  $P_{j_{\lambda}^*}$  in its interaction with  $P_{i^*}$ ,  $\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}$  forwards these to a challenger on the right, and in return obtains receiver messages on behalf of  $P_{i^*}$ .

In other words,  $MIM_{\lambda}$ 's interaction with its challengers generates either the random variable (defined in Definition 7.1)

$$\mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}} \langle \{ \mathcal{C}(c_{i^*}, r_{i^*,j}) \}_{j \in \mathbb{S}}, \mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}), \mathcal{R} \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}) \rangle (1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{tag}_{i^*}, \mathsf{ta$$

or the random variable

$$\mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}}\langle\{\mathcal{C}(0^{k(\lambda)},0^{\lambda})\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}},\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}),\mathcal{R}\rangle(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{tag}_{i^{*}},\mathsf{tag}_{j^{*}_{\lambda}}),$$

depending on which strings the challenger on the left is committing to.

We now show the existence of a quantum polynomial-time  $D = \{D_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  that succeeds in distinguishing these distributions with non-negligible advantage, which contradicts the non-malleability of nmCom as defined in Definition 7.1. The distribution received by  $D_{\lambda}$  includes the message  $(c^*, r^*)$  committed by  $MIM_{\lambda}$  in its interaction with  $P_{i^*}$  on the right, along with the final view VIEW of  $MIM_{\lambda}$ , which includes  $A^*$ 's view after Step 2 of Protocol 6. It then simulates the remainder of the coin-flipping protocol in the presence of  $A^*_{\lambda}$ , with one difference. Instead of implementing the check introduced in  $\mathcal{H}_1$ , it checks only that  $y_{j^*_{\lambda}} \in \mathcal{L}^{(\text{both})}$ , using the message  $(c^*, r^*)$ . If this check failed and there was no abort, it outputs 1 and otherwise outputs 0.

Now observe that

$$\begin{split} &\Pr\left[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda}(\mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}}\langle\{\mathcal{C}(c_{i^*},r_{i^*,j})\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}},\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}),\mathcal{R}\rangle(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{tag}_{i^*},\mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}})) = 1\right] \\ &= \Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_{2,j^*_{\lambda}}] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_{2}] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda), \text{ and} \\ &\Pr\left[\mathsf{D}_{\lambda}(\mathsf{View-Val}_{\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}}\langle\{\mathcal{C}(0^{k(\lambda)},0^{\lambda})\}_{j\in\mathbb{S}},\mathsf{MIM}_{\lambda}(\rho_{\lambda}),\mathcal{R}\rangle(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{tag}_{i^*},\mathsf{tag}_{j^*_{\lambda}})) = 1\right] \\ &= \Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_{3,j^*_{\lambda}}] \geq 1/p'(\lambda), \end{split}$$

which establishes that D has a non-negligible advantage, a contradiction.

•  $\mathcal{H}_3 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4$ : This follows from the quantum zero-knowledge of ZK. The non-uniform advice given to the malicious verifier derived from A\* will include the transcript of the first two rounds of Protocol 6 executed with adversary A\*, along with the openings (if they exist) of the commitments made by A\* in Step 2. The final view of this verifier will also include these openings, allowing the reduction to efficiently simulate the remainder of the protocol, in particular using these openings to efficiently implement the check introduced in  $\mathcal{H}_1$ .

{66}------------------------------------------------

•  $\mathcal{H}_4 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_5$ : We consider a sequence of sub-hybrids  $\mathcal{H}_{4,0}, \ldots, \mathcal{H}_{4,|\mathbb{S}|}$ . Associate the set  $\mathbb{S}$  with the set  $[1,|\mathbb{S}|]$ , and define  $\mathcal{H}_{4,j}$  so that in Step 3,  $P_{i^*}$  commits to  $0^{k(\lambda)}$  when interacting with adversarial parties  $P_k$  for  $k \leq j$  and commits to  $c_{i^*}$  when interacting with adversarial parties  $P_k$  for k > j. Observe that  $\mathcal{H}_4 = \mathcal{H}_{4,0}$  and  $\mathcal{H}_5 = \mathcal{H}_{4,|\mathbb{S}|}$ .

We now show that for any  $j \in [1, \mathbb{S}]$ , the indistinguishability  $\mathcal{H}_{4,j-1} \approx_c \mathcal{H}_{4,j}$  follows from the quantum computational hiding of eCom. Indeed, define a receiver  $\mathsf{R}^* = (R_\lambda^*, \rho_\lambda)$  that interacts with a single committer committing to either  $c_{i^*}$  or  $0^{k(\lambda)}$  as follows. It takes as non-uniform advice  $\rho_\lambda$  the transcript of the first two rounds of Protocol 6 executed with adversary  $\mathsf{A}_\lambda^*$ , along with the openings (if they exist) of the commitments made by  $\mathsf{A}_\lambda^*$  in Step 2. It then simulates the remainder of the protocol, interacting with the challenger to implement  $P_{i^*}$ 's messages in Step 3 during the eCom session when  $P_j$  is the receiver. Observe that  $\mathsf{R}^*$  can indeed efficiently simulate the entire protocol, in particular it can implement the check introduced in  $\mathcal{H}_1$  since it has the openings to the commitments given by  $\mathsf{A}_\lambda^*$  in Step 2. Any efficient distinguisher that distinguishes between  $\mathcal{H}_{4,j-1}$  and  $\mathcal{H}_{4,j}$  with non-negligible advantage immediately implies that  $\mathsf{R}^*$  distinguishes with non-negligible advantage, breaking quantum computational hiding of eCom.

- $\mathcal{H}_5 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_6$ : Same argument as  $\mathcal{H}_3 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_4$ .
- $\mathcal{H}_6 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_7$ : Assume that there exists a distinguisher D that can distinguish between the outputs of these hybrids with non-negligible advantage. We build a compliant<sup>20</sup> distinguisher D' that breaks the extractability property of eCom.

D' will receive as non-uniform advice i) the transcript of the first two rounds of Protocol 6 executed with adversary A\*, ii) the state of A\* at this point, and iii) the openings (if they exist) of the commitments made by *each* party in Step 2. Note that the non-uniform advice given to the committer C\* defined in the description of SimNoAbort\_ is a strict subset of this advice. Now, D' will forward this subset (which consists of the state of A\* and the commitment openings of parties  $\{P_i\}_{i\in\mathbb{H}}$ ) to its challenger, and receive either the real or simulated view with respect to committer C\*. It can then efficiently generate the rest of the distribution using its non-uniform advice and the view it received from the challenger, additionally returning an abort if the messages  $\{c_i'\}_{i\in\mathbb{S}}$  it received as part of the challenge distribution do not match the messages  $\{c_i'\}_{i\in\mathbb{S}}$  broadcast in Step 5. If during Step 4, any of the parties  $\{P_i\}_{i\in\mathbb{S}}$  fails to prove it ZK statement, D' outputs 0. Otherwise, it queries D with the final distribution and outputs what D outputs.

It remains to show that i) D''s advantage is negligibly close to D's advantage, and ii) D' is compliant. The first point requires two observations. First, whenever D' does *not* query D, it means that A\* failed to prove one of it ZK statements, so D's input would have been  $\bot$  anyway. Next, we need to show that when D' *does* query D with a transcript, it is a faithful execution of either  $\mathcal{H}_6$  or  $\mathcal{H}_7$ , depending on whether eCom was simulated or not. If eCom was simulated, the distribution is equivalent to  $\mathcal{H}_7$ . If not, the distribution is equivalent to  $\mathcal{H}_6$ , except for the extra abort condition carried out by the reduction. However, observe that the probability that the reduction produces an abort but  $\mathcal{H}_6$  does not is at most  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_6]$ . Since  $\mathcal{H}_6 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_1$ , and  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_1] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , this quantity is negligible. Now it remains to argue that D' is compliant, but this follows directly from the quantum computational soundness of ZK.

•  $\mathcal{H}_7 \approx_s \mathcal{H}_8$ : First,  $\Pr[\mathsf{BAD}_7] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , since  $\mathcal{H}_7 \approx_c \mathcal{H}_1$ . Next, switching  $P_{i^*}$ 's message in Step 4 is perfectly indistinguishable since r is uniformly random.

Now, note that  $\mathsf{RealAbort}_{\perp}(\mathsf{A}^*) \approx_c \mathsf{SimAbort}_{\perp}(\mathsf{A}^*)$  follows by definition. Then, it follows identically to the proof of Theorem 6.1 that  $\mathsf{Real}(\mathsf{A}^*) \approx_c \mathsf{SimComb}(\mathsf{A}^*)$ , and then by applying Lemma 3.1, that  $\mathsf{Real}(\mathsf{A}^*) \approx_c \mathsf{Sim}(\mathsf{A}^*)$ .

<span id="page-66-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>20</sup>Recall that such a distinguisher is guaranteed to output 0 with overwhelming probability on input any non-explainable view.

{67}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-67-0"></span>9 Quantum-Secure Multi-Party Computation

#### <span id="page-67-1"></span>9.1 Definition

We follow the standard real/ideal world paradigm for defining secure multi-party computation (MPC) as in [Gol04], replacing classical adversaries with quantum adversaries.

Consider n parties  $P_1,\ldots,P_n$  with inputs  $x_1,\ldots,x_n$  that wish to interact in a protocol  $\Pi$  to evaluate any functionality f on their joint inputs. The security of protocol  $\Pi$  (with respect to a functionality f) is defined by comparing the real-world execution of the protocol with an ideal-world evaluation of f by a trusted party. Informally, it is required that for every quantum adversary  $A = \{A_\lambda\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  that corrupts some subset of the parties  $I \subset [n]$  and participates in the real execution of the protocol, there exists an adversary A simplifies a simulator, that can *achieve the same effect* in the ideal world. In fact, we provide a strictly stronger definition that allows the adversary A some arbitrary non-uniform quantum advice  $\{\rho_\lambda\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$  thay may even depend on the inputs  $x_1,\ldots,x_n$ .

We now formally describe the security definition, which only considers the case of fully *malicious* adversaries. Let  $\vec{x} = (x_1, \dots, x_n)$  be the set of inputs.

**The Real Execution**. In the real execution, the n-party protocol  $\Pi$  for computing f is executed in the presence of a quantum polynomial-time adversary  $A = \{A_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ , where A corrupts some set  $I \subset [n]$  of the parties. The honest parties follow the instructions of  $\Pi$ , and A sends all messages of the protocol on behalf of the corrupted parties following any arbitrary quantum polynomial-time strategy.

The interaction of  $(A_{\lambda}, \rho_{\lambda})$  in the protocol  $\Pi$  defines a random variable REAL $_{\Pi,A}(\lambda, \vec{x}, \rho_{\lambda})$  whose value is determined by the randomness of the adversary and the honest parties. This random variable contains the output of the adversary (which may be an arbitrary function of its view and in particular may be a quantum state) as well as the outputs of the honest parties.

**The Ideal Execution**. In the ideal execution, an ideal world adversary Sim interacts with a trusted party, as follows.

- Send inputs to the trusted party: Each honest party sends its input to the trusted party. Each corrupt party  $P_i$ , (controlled by Sim) may either send its input  $x_i$ , or send some other input of the same length to the trusted party. Let  $x_i'$  denote the value sent by party  $P_i$ .
- Trusted party sends output to the adversary: The trusted party computes  $f(x'_1, \ldots, x'_n) = (y_1, \ldots, y_n)$  and sends  $\{y_i\}_{i \in I}$  to the adversary.
- Adversary instructs trusted party to abort or continue: This is formalized by having the adversary send either an abort or continue message to the trusted party. In the latter case, the trusted party sends to each honest party  $P_i$  its output value  $y_i$ . In the former case, the trusted party sends the special symbol  $\bot$  to each honest party.
- Outputs: Sim outputs an arbitrary function of its view, and the honest parties output the values obtained from the trusted party.

The interaction of Sim with the trusted party defines a random variable IDEAL $_{f,Sim}(\lambda, \vec{x}, \rho_{\lambda})$ . Having defined the real and the ideal worlds, we now proceed to define our notion of security.

**Definition 9.1.** Let f be an n-party functionality, and  $\Pi$  be an n-party protocol. Protocol  $\Pi$  securely computes f if for every quantum polynomial-time real-world adversary  $A = \{A_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  corrupting a set of at most n-1 players, there exists a quantum polynomial-time ideal-world adversary S im such that for any set of inputs  $\vec{x} \in (\{0,1\}^*)^n$  and any non-uniform quantum advice  $\rho = \{\rho_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$ ,

$$\{\mathsf{REAL}_{\Pi,\mathsf{A}}(\lambda,\vec{x},\rho_{\lambda})\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}} \approx_{c} \{\mathsf{IDEAL}_{f,\mathsf{Sim}}(\lambda,\vec{x},\rho_{\lambda})\}_{\lambda\in\mathbb{N}}.$$

{68}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-68-0"></span>**9.2 Construction**

Given the construction of quantum-secure multi-party coin-flipping from Section [8,](#page-59-0) it is straightforward to achieve quantum-secure multi-party computation, due to the following lemma adapted from [\[KOS03\]](#page-73-10). For completeness, we give a sketch of the proof.

**Lemma 9.1.** *Given a quantum-secure multi-party coin-flipping protocol and a quantum-secure protocol* Π *for computing* f *in the common random string (CRS) model with straight-line black-box simulation, the natural composition of the two is a quantum-secure protocol for computing* f *with no setup assumptions.*

*Proof.* (sketch) Consider any adversary (Aλ, ρλ) for the composed protocol. A<sup>λ</sup> may be split into two parts: A<sup>1</sup> interacts in the coin-flipping protocol and produces a state st, which is passed to A2, who interacts in Π. We now construct a simulator Sim for the composed protocol as follows. It begins by running the straight-line black-box simulator Sim<sup>Π</sup> for Π until it outputs a CRS r (note that since Sim<sup>Π</sup> is straight-line, this CRS-generation step is independent of the adversary and advice, and does not require a call to the ideal functionality). At this point, Sim runs the simulator for the multi-party coin-flipping protocol on input r, adversary A1, and non-uniform advice ρλ. This simulation produces a final state st. Finally, Sim completes the execution of Sim<sup>Π</sup> on input A2(st) and outputs what Sim<sup>Π</sup> outputs.
